Testimony Required to Prove Birth of a Woman in Iddah
Ruling on testimony required to prove a woman in iddah gave birth

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions regarding the number of witnesses required to prove a woman in iddah gave birth, identify the reasoning (talil) of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn, and explain the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf).

Core Idea
When a woman in iddah claims birth and the husband denies, Abu Hanifa requires full testimony because the iddah ends; the Sahibayn accept one midwife because the bed remains.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq examines a dispute within the Hanafi school on the proof of childbirth for a woman in iddah. The core question is whether the marital bed (firash) continues or ends upon the woman's claim of delivery, which determines the level of testimony needed. Understanding this issue sharpens your ability to apply principles of evidence in family law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal issue
What specific ruling is being disputed? It concerns the number and gender of witnesses required to prove that a woman in iddah gave birth when the husband or his heirs deny the birth.
Focus on the intra-Hanafi difference: Abu Hanifa vs. the Sahibayn.
2
State the position of Abu Hanifa
Abu Hanifa requires complete testimony (shahādat kāmilah) – two men or one man and two women. His reasoning is that the iddah ends with the woman’s admission of delivery, so the marital bed is removed and lineage must be proven anew.
Note the term 'shahādat kāmilah' – it is the standard for most legal proofs.
3
State the position of the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad)
The two disciples accept the testimony of a single qualified midwife. Their reasoning is that the iddah continues until the birth is actually verified, so the marital bed remains, and only confirmation of the birth is needed.
Their view relies on the principle that the bed is established by the iddah.
4
Analyse the talil (causative reasoning)
The difference stems from the legal status of the iddah after the woman claims delivery. Abu Hanifa considers the iddah ended by her mere claim, so proof is from scratch. The Sahibayn treat the iddah as continuing until actual verification, so the bed persists and less proof suffices.
This is a classic dispute over the effect of a claim on legal continuity.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: if the husband denies, Abu Hanifa requires two male witnesses or one man and two women; the Sahibayn require only one midwife. The mufta bihi (operative view) is Abu Hanifa’s.
The reliance on Abu Hanifa’s view is the standard for fatwa in the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمعتدة: أي يثبت نسب ولد المعتدة بالشهادة الكاملة إن جحد الزوج أو الورثة ولادتها عند أبي حنيفة، وقالا: يثبت بشهادة امرأة واحدة قابلة لقيام الفراش بقيام العدة، وعند الشافعي بشهادة أربع من النساء وعند مالك: بشهادة امرأتين ولأبي حنيفة أن العدة تنقضي بإقرارها بوضع الحمل فزال الفراش والمنقضي لا يكون حجة، فمست الحاجة إلى إثبات النسب ابتداء، فيشترط فيه كمال الحجة ثم قيل: تقبل شهادة رجلين، ولا يفسقان بالنظر إلى العورة، إما أنه يتفق ذلك من غير قصد نظر ولا تعمد أو للضرورة كما في شهود الزنا، على أن الشهادة قد تكون بالنظر كما إذا دخلت بيتاً بحضرتهم يعلمون أنه ليس فيها غيرها ثم خرجت ومعها ولد فيعلمون أنها ولدته. رمز الحقائق: ٣٠٥/١]
And the woman in iddah: i.e., the lineage of the child of the woman in iddah is established by complete testimony if the husband or the heirs deny her giving birth according to Abu Hanifa. And the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: it is established by the testimony of a single qualified female midwife because the marital bed is established through the iddah. According to al-Shafi'i, by the testimony of four women; according to Malik, by two women. Abu Hanifa's argument is that the iddah is ended by her admission of giving birth, so the marital bed is removed, and what has ended cannot be evidence, so the need arises to establish lineage anew, thus complete proof is required. Then it is said: the testimony of two men is accepted, and they do not become sinners by looking at the private parts, because either it happens without intent or due to necessity, as in witnesses to zina, considering that testimony may be based on sight as when she enters a house in their presence knowing no one else is there, then she comes out with a child, and they know she gave birth.
Source pages p. 515

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute on the number and gender of witnesses needed to prove a woman in iddah gave birth when the husband (or his heirs) denies it. According to Abu Hanifa, complete testimony is required: two men or one man and two women, because the iddah is considered to have ended by her claim of delivery, so the marital bed is no longer in effect, and lineage must be proven from scratch, requiring full legal proof. However, the two companions (Sahibayn) hold that the testimony of one trustworthy midwife is sufficient because the marital bed still exists as long as the iddah continues, and the midwife's testimony supports the presumption of continuity. The commentary also notes the views of al-Shafi'i (four women) and Malik (two women). Abu Hanifa justifies the validity of male witnesses viewing the birth by necessity, similar to witnesses of zina, or if the birth can be inferred without directly viewing the private parts.

Key Terms
الشهادة الكاملة (al-shahādat al-kāmilah): Complete testimony: two men or one man and two women, as required for most legal proofs in Hanafi fiqh.
القابلة (al-qābilah): A midwife; a woman who assists in childbirth and can testify to the birth.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A woman in iddah gives birth, and her husband denies it. According to Abu Hanifa, what testimony is required to prove the birth and establish the child's lineage?
A) Testimony of one midwife.
B) Testimony of four women.
C) Testimony of two men or one man and two women.
D) Testimony of one man and one woman.

Key Takeaway
When the husband denies birth during iddah, the Hanafi school (mufta bihi) requires complete testimony (two men or one man and two women) to establish lineage.

Lineage of Child When a Woman Claims Her 'Iddah Ended
Ruling on lineage for a woman who admits her 'iddah is over but gives birth

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the lineage of a child when a woman claims her 'iddah has ended, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
A woman's claim of completed 'iddah is overridden if she gives birth within six months.

This passage addresses a scenario where a divorced or widowed woman declares that her 'iddah (waiting period) has expired. However, she then gives birth. The question is: does the child belong to her former husband? The answer depends on when she gives birth relative to her admission. The Hanafi school draws a clear line based on a six-month period, which reveals the truth of her claim or its falsehood.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key scenario
Pinpoint the situation: a woman (muqirrah) claims her 'iddah is over, then gives birth. Determine the timeframes involved: from her admission and from the separation.
2
State the Hanafi ruling
If she gives birth within six months of her admission, the child's lineage to the former husband is established. If after six months, it is not established.
Also note that the birth must occur within two years of the separation for the lineage to be established if within six months of admission.
3
Analyze the proof (dalil)
The text states 'li dhuhur kadhibiha bi-yaqin' (because her lie becomes apparent with certainty). Explain why: if her 'iddah were truly over, she could not be pregnant from the husband; a pregnancy visible at birth within six months confirms she was pregnant during the 'iddah, so her claim was false.
4
Examine the Shafi'i objection and Hanafi response
Shafi'i argues for favorable interpretation (haml 'ala al-salah): assume she told the truth and the child is from a subsequent marriage. The Hanafi response: the short timeframe makes a second marriage highly unlikely, and invalidating the husband's right of return is permissible when the her statement is shown false.
The Hanafi position is based on certainty of falsehood, not mere suspicion.
5
Apply the ruling
Practice: If a woman says 'my 'iddah ended on 1 Muharram' and gives birth on 1 Rajab (6 months later), lineage is not established because it is exactly six months? Actually less than six months from admission? Clarify: if she gives birth on 1 Rajab, that is exactly six months? The text says 'less than six months' – so if exactly six months, it is not less? Consider the Hanafi rule: a pregnancy can be as short as six months? The text implies that if she gives birth at six months or more, the admission is not contradicted. So exactly six months would be borderline? Usually, minimum pregnancy is 6 months, so birth at 6 months is possible from before the 'iddah ended. Thus, the ruling: if birth occurs at exactly six months or more, lineage not established. The text says 'less than six months' for establishment, so at six months it is not established. This is important.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمقرة إلخ: بالجر أيضاً أي يثبت نسب ولد المقرة بمضي العدة إذا ولدت لأقل من ستة أشهر من وقت الإقرار سواء كانت كبيرة أو مراهقة وسواء كانت العدة عدة الطلاق أو الوفات لظهور كذبها بيقين، وهذا إذا جاءت به لأقل من سنتين من وقت الفراق، وإن جاءت به لأكثر منهما لا يثبت وإن كان أقل . ستة أشهر من الإقرار. رمز الحقائق: ٣٠٤/١] (فتح) بمضيها بمضي العدة سواء كانت كبيرة أو لا، وسواء كانت العدة عدة طلاق أو وفات (ط) وإلا لا: أي وإن لم تلد لستة أشهر من وقت الإقرار بل ولدت لأكثر لا يثبت نسبه منه وعند الشافعي: يثبت؛ لأن حمل أمرها على الصلاح ممكن فوجب الحمل عليه، وفي ضده حمله على الزنا، وفيه إضرار على الولد بإبطال حقه في النسب فيرد إقرارها ولنا أنها أمينة في الإخبار فيقبل قولها، ولا يلزم من قطعه عند كونه عن الزنا لاحتمال أنها تزوجت بغيره على أن إبطال حق الغير بقول الأمين جائز إذا لم يكن كذباً شرعاً، ألا ترى أنها تصدق في انقضاء عدتها بالإقرار وأن تضمن إبطال حق الزوج في الرجعة. (فتح، عيني)
And the woman who admits, etc.: also in the genitive, meaning the lineage of the child of a woman who admits to the passing of her 'iddah is established if she gives birth within less than six months from the time of her admission, whether she is an adult or a minor approaching puberty, and whether the 'iddah is due to divorce or death, because her lie becomes apparent with certainty. This is if she brings the child within less than two years from the time of separation. If she brings it after more than that, it is not established even if less than six months from the admission. (Rami al-Haqaiq 1/304) (Fath) ... And if not: meaning if she does not give birth within six months from the time of admission but gives birth after more, his lineage is not established from him. According to al-Shafi'i: it is established because interpreting her matter as righteous is possible, so it must be interpreted that way. The opposite would interpret her as committing adultery, which harms the child by invalidating his right to lineage, so her admission would be rejected. Our proof: she is trustworthy in her report, so her statement is accepted. It does not necessarily mean the child is from adultery because it is possible she married another man. Also, invalidating another's right by the statement of a trustworthy person is permissible if it is not legally considered a lie. Do you not see that she is believed regarding the completion of her 'iddah by admission, even though it may invalidate the husband's right to take her back? (Fath, Ayni)
Source pages p. 515

Commentary
This passage expands on the case of a woman who admits (muqirrah) that her 'iddah has passed. The ruling: if she gives birth within six months of her admission, the child's lineage to her former husband is established, because her claim of completed 'iddah is proven false by the birth (since if the 'iddah were truly over, she could not be pregnant from him). If she gives birth after six months, her admission is taken as true, and the child is not from him. The commentary also includes a dispute with al-Shafi'i, who argues that one should interpret her situation favorably, assuming the child is from a second marriage. The Hanafi response: her trustworthiness (amanah) is limited; when the birth occurs within six months, it reveals the falsity of her admission, so she is not believed regarding the 'iddah's end. Moreover, even if she were believed, it would not necessarily harm the child's lineage because the child could be from a subsequent marriage, but the short time frame makes that highly unlikely.

Key Terms
مقرة بمضي العدة (muqirrah bi-imdha' al-'iddah): A woman who declares that her 'iddah period has ended.
إقرار (iqrar): Admission or confession of a fact, here specifically that the 'iddah has passed.
نسب (nasab): Lineage or paternity; the legal relationship between a child and his father.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) When the birth occurs within six months of her admission and within two years of separation
B) When the birth occurs after six months of her admission
C) When the woman is a minor
D) When the 'iddah was due to death

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi ruling on a woman's claim of 'iddah's end is balanced: her statement is accepted unless a birth within six months proves it false with certainty.

Testimony for Proving Birth of Mu'taddah: Intra-Hanafi and Inter-Madhhab Dispute
Intra-Hanafi and inter-madhhab dispute over testimony

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the different testimony requirements for proving birth of a mu'taddah according to Abu Hanifa, the Sahibayn, and the other schools, and apply the concept of firash to judge which testimony suffices.

Core Idea
The marital bed (firash) determines the level of proof: Abu Hanifa requires full testimony, while the Sahibayn accept a midwife because firash persists during 'iddah.

This passage addresses a classic scenario: a woman in 'iddah claims she gave birth, but the husband denies it. How do we establish lineage? The answer hinges on whether the marital bed (firash) is considered present. Abu Hanifa and his disciples disagree, and the Shafi'i and Maliki schools add further nuance.

Guided Path
1
State the legal scenario
A woman is in 'iddah from a revocable or irrevocable divorce. She claims delivery of a child. The husband (or heirs) denies the birth. The question is: what testimony is required to prove the child's lineage?
The child's lineage is critical for inheritance and paternity.
2
Identify Abu Hanifa's position and proof
Abu Hanifa requires complete testimony (shahadah kamilah: two men or one man and two women). His proof: the woman's claim of delivery ends the 'iddah, removing the marital bed (firash). Therefore, lineage must be established anew, requiring full evidence.
The removal of firash is key: without it, the child's lineage is not automatically attached.
3
Identify the Sahibayn's position and proof
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad say the testimony of a single midwife woman (qabilah) is sufficient. Their proof: the 'iddah continues until it is proven to have ended (e.g., by sight of the child). The marital bed remains, so the usual reduced testimony for matters related to women and childbirth applies.
This is an exception to the general rule of testimony based on necessity and the presence of firash.
4
Note the Shafi'i and Maliki positions
Imam al-Shafi'i requires four women for testimony, while Imam Malik requires two women. Both differ from the Hanafi views.
These positions are based on their schools' rules on female testimony in sensitive matters.
5
Synthesize the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference is the number of witnesses needed. Under Abu Hanifa, two men (or one man plus two women) are needed; under the Sahibayn, one midwife is enough; under Shafi'i, four women; under Maliki, two women.
This directly affects the feasibility of proving lineage in such cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمعتدة: أي يثبت نسب ولد المعتدة بالشهادة الكاملة إن جحد الزوج أو الورثة ولادتها عند أبي حنيفة، وقالا: يثبت بشهادة امرأة واحدة قابلة لقيام الفراش بقيام العدة، وعند الشافعي بشهادة أربع من النساء وعند مالك: بشهادة امرأتين ولأبي حنيفة أن العدة تنقضي بإقرارها بوضع الحمل فزال الفراش والمنقضي لا يكون حجة، فمست الحاجة إلى إثبات النسب ابتداء، فيشترط فيه كمال الحجة ثم قيل: تقبل شهادة رجلين، ولا يفسقان بالنظر إلى العورة، إما أنه يتفق ذلك من غير قصد نظر ولا تعمد أو للضرورة كما في شهود الزنا، على أن الشهادة قد تكون بالنظر كما إذا دخلت بيتاً بحضرتهم يعلمون أنه ليس فيها غيرها ثم خرجت ومعها ولد فيعلمون أنها ولدته. رمز الحقائق: ٣٠٥/١]
The mu'taddah: meaning the lineage of the child of the mu'taddah is established by complete testimony if the husband or the heirs deny her giving birth according to Abu Hanifa. The Two Disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say: it is established by the testimony of a single midwife woman, due to the existence of the marital bed (firash) by the existence of the 'iddah. According to al-Shafi'i, by the testimony of four women, and according to Malik, by the testimony of two women. Abu Hanifa's proof: the 'iddah ends by her admission of delivery, so the marital bed is removed, and what has ended cannot be evidence. So there is a need to establish lineage anew, so complete evidence is required. Then it is said: the testimony of two men is accepted, and they are not considered sinners for looking at the private parts, either because it happens without intent or out of necessity, as in witnesses of adultery. Moreover, testimony may be by sight, as if she enters a house in their presence and they know there is no one else inside, then she emerges with a child, so they know she gave birth. (Rami al-Haqaiq 1/305)
Source pages p. 515

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the matn regarding the mu'taddah whose delivery is denied by the husband. It presents the different opinions: Abu Hanifa requires full testimony (shahadah kamilah: two men or one man+two women) because he considers that once the woman claims delivery, the 'iddah ends and the marital bed is lifted, so lineage must be established anew. The Two Disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say a single midwife's testimony suffices because the marital bed remains until the 'iddah is actually proven to have ended. Shafi'i requires four women, Maliki two women. The passage also addresses the issue of witnesses looking at the private parts during childbirth; it is excused due to necessity or inadvertence. Also, testimony can be based on sight (e.g., seeing her enter alone and exit with a child).

Key Terms
شهادة كاملة (shahadah kamilah): Complete testimony: two men or one man and two women.
فراش (firash): Marital bed, the legal relationship that establishes paternity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, what testimony is required to prove the birth of a child for a woman in 'iddah when the husband denies the delivery?
A) A single midwife woman
B) Two men or one man and two women
C) Four women
D) Two women

Key Takeaway
The level of testimony required to prove birth for a mu'taddah depends on whether the marital bed (firash) is considered present or absent after her claim of delivery.

Establishing Lineage Before Birth: Visible Pregnancy and Husband's Acknowledgment
Ruling on establishment of lineage before birth through two alternative methods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two means by which lineage of a child of a mu'taddah is established before birth and apply the definition of visible pregnancy to hypothetical scenarios.

Core Idea
Lineage of a mu'taddah's child is established before birth by visible pregnancy with midwife testimony or by husband's acknowledgment.

In Hanafi law, the lineage of a child born to a woman in her 'iddah (waiting period after divorce) can be established even before the child is born. This lesson explores two specific methods: a clearly visible pregnancy attested by a midwife, and the husband's own acknowledgment of the pregnancy. Understanding these rules protects the child's right to paternal lineage, inheritance, and identity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal subject
The ruling applies to the child of a mu'taddah (a woman in her waiting period after a revocable or irrevocable divorce). The goal is to establish nasab (lineage) linking the child to the husband.
The context is limited to children born during the 'iddah period.
2
Recognize the two alternative methods
The text provides two independent means: (1) visible pregnancy (hubl zahir) with the testimony of a midwife, and (2) the husband's acknowledgment (iqrar) of the pregnancy. Either is sufficient by consensus.
These are alternatives, not cumulative conditions.
3
Define 'visible pregnancy'
The passage defines hubl zahir as pregnancy signs so clear that anyone who sees the woman would strongly believe she is pregnant. This requires objective, observable evidence.
Subjective claims or early-stage pregnancy without visible signs do not qualify.
4
Understand the role of consensus
The text notes that the first method (visible pregnancy with midwife testimony) is established 'by consensus' (bil-ittifaq), indicating that all major scholars agree on this ruling.
This strengthens the authority of the ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو حبل ظاهر: أي يثبت نسب ولد المعتدة بشهادة القابلة مع وجود حبل ظاهر بالاتفاق، والمراد بظهور الحبل أن تكون أمارات حملها بالغة مبلغاً يوجب غلبة الظن كونها حاملاً لكل من شاهدها، وقوله: "أو إقراره به" أي يثبت أيضاً بإقرار من الزوج بالحبل؛ لأن النسب في هذين ثابت قبل الولادة. (فتح)

The meaning of visibility of pregnancy is that the signs of her pregnancy have reached a level that makes it highly probable to everyone who sees her that she is pregnant. And his statement: 'or his acknowledgment of it' means it is also established by the husband's acknowledgment of the pregnancy, because lineage in these two cases is established before birth. (Fath)
Source pages p. 516

Commentary
This short passage covers two additional means of establishing lineage for a child of a mu'taddah. First, if there is a visible pregnancy (hubl zahir), attested by a midwife, lineage is established by consensus. Visible pregnancy is defined as signs so clear that anyone seeing her would strongly believe she is pregnant. Second, if the husband himself acknowledges the pregnancy, the child's lineage is established. In both cases, lineage is considered established even before the child is born.

Key Terms
حبل ظاهر (hubl zahir): A pregnancy that is visibly discernible to observers, with clear signs that make it highly probable.
إقرار (iqrar): Acknowledgment, here by the husband that his wife is pregnant by him.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the lineage of a mu'taddah's child is secured before birth through objective visible pregnancy with midwife testimony or through the husband's own acknowledgment.

Establishing Lineage by Heirs' Confirmation
Ruling on lineage through heirs' confirmation with istihsan

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rule for establishing lineage through heirs' confirmation, explain the istihsan basis, and distinguish between the two aspects of birth establishment and child identification.

Core Idea
Heirs' confirmation establishes lineage by istihsan — they stand in the deceased's place.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq addresses a practical scenario: a widow in 'iddah of death gives birth, and the deceased's heirs confirm her claim. The ruling uses istihsan to override strict analogy, allowing lineage to be established without formal testimony. However, the identification of the child always requires a midwife. A related intra-Hanafi disagreement on the woman's own statement for establishing birth is also explained.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core scenario
Recognise that the case involves a widow in 'iddah of death and the heirs confirming her childbirth account. Note that their confirmation (tasdiq) suffices even without formal testimony.
The heirs may be all or some of them.
2
Understand the proof via istihsan
Qiyas would reject this because lineage is being attributed to the deceased without direct evidence. Istihsan accepts it because the heirs stand in the place of the deceased (qā’imūn maqām al-mayyit), so their acceptance is given weight.
This is a classic example of istihsan overriding qiyas.
3
Separate the two dimensions of the case
The passage distinguishes between establishing the birth itself (nash’at al-wilādah) and identifying the child (ta‘yīn al-walad). The former is disputed; the latter requires a midwife by consensus.
The dispute is between Abu Hanifa and the Two Disciples on birth establishment.
4
Apply the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
The difference appears in conditional divorces or emancipations: if the husband said 'If she gives birth, she is divorced,' Abu Hanifa accepts her statement, while the Two Disciples require a midwife's testimony.
This shows how the disagreement affects actual rulings.
5
Summarise the complete ruling
Lineage is established by heirs' confirmation (by istihsan). Child identification needs a midwife (consensus). Birth establishment: Abu Hanifa accepts woman's word; Two Disciples require a midwife.
The mufta bihi is not specified in the text; both views are noted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو تصديق الورثة : [ بالجر عطف على قوله بشهادة رجلين أي يثبت نسب ولد المعتدة عن وفات بتصديق الورثة كلهم أو بعضهم. (ع)] أي يثبت نسب ولد المعتدة عن وفات أن يصدق الورثة ما قالت المرأة ولم يشهدوا به، وهذا الثبوت في حق الإرث ظاهر؛ لأنه خالص حقهم، ويثبت في حق غيرهم أيضاً استحساناً، وإن كان القياس يأباه لما فيه من حمل النسب على الغير وهو الميت وجه الاستحسان أنهم قائمون مقام الميت فيقبل قولهم، ثم اعلم أنه لابد من شهادة القابلة لتعين الولد إجماعاً في جميع هذه الصور، والخلاف في ثبوت نفس الولادة بقول المعتدة، فعند أبي حنيفة: يثبت به في الصور الثلاث وعندهما لا يثبت إلا بشهادة القابلة وأما نسب الولد فلا يثبت بالإجماع إلا بشهادة القابلة؛ لاحتمال أن يكون هو غير هذا المعين، وثمرة الخلاف لا تظهر إلا في حق حكم آخر كالطلاق والعتاق بأن علقها بولادتها حتى يقع عند أبي حنيفة بقولها: ولدت؛ لأنها أمينة لاعترافه بالحمل أو لظهوره فيقبل قولها، وعندهما: لا يقع شيء حتى تشهد قابلة [ رمز الحقائق: ٣٠٥/١] (فتح)
Or attestation of the heirs: [in the genitive, conjoined to his statement 'by the testimony of two men', meaning the lineage of the child of the widow in 'iddah of death is established by the attestation of the heirs, all or some of them. (Ayni)] meaning the lineage of the child of the widow in 'iddah of death is established if the heirs confirm what the woman said, even if they did not testify to it. This establishment is obvious regarding inheritance because it is purely their right. It is also established regarding others by istihsan, even though qiyas would oppose it because it involves attributing lineage to another, i.e., the deceased. The istihsan justification is that they stand in the place of the deceased, so their statement is accepted. Then know that testimony of a midwife is necessary to identify the child by consensus in all these forms. The disagreement is about establishing the birth itself by the woman's statement. According to Abu Hanifa, it is established by her in the three cases (death, divorce, etc.), while according to them (the Two Disciples), it is not established except by the testimony of a midwife. As for the lineage of the child, it is not established by consensus except by the testimony of a midwife, because it is possible that this child is not the specific one. The fruit of the disagreement only appears in another ruling, such as a conditional divorce or emancipation, e.g., if a man says: 'if she gives birth, she is divorced', then according to Abu Hanifa, it occurs by her statement 'I gave birth' because she is trustworthy due to his acknowledgment of the pregnancy or its visibility, so her statement is accepted. According to them, nothing occurs until a midwife testifies. (Rami al-Haqaiq 1/305) (Fath)
Source pages p. 516

Commentary
This passage covers the case where the heirs of a deceased husband confirm (tasdiq) the widow's account of childbirth. Even if they did not witness the birth, their confirmation is sufficient to establish lineage. This is by istihsan, overriding qiyas because the heirs stand in the place of the deceased. However, a midwife's testimony is still needed to identify the specific child by consensus. The passage also details a related intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Hanifa accepts the woman's own statement regarding the birth (e.g., for conditional divorces) because she is trustworthy, while the Two Disciples require a midwife's testimony.

Key Terms
تصديق الورثة (tasdiq al-wurath): The heirs' confirmation or attestation that the widow's account of childbirth is true, without them testifying formally.
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference, a branch of qiyas khafi, used to override strict analogy for a more equitable outcome.
قائمون مقام الميت (qā'imūn maqām al-mayyit): Standing in the place of the deceased; the legal principle that heirs take the position of the deceased in certain matters.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Midwife's testimony by consensus
C) The woman's own statement
D) Testimony of two male witnesses

Key Takeaway
Heirs' confirmation, even without testimony, establishes lineage by istihsan, but identification of the child always requires a midwife by consensus.

Establishing Lineage of a Child Born to a Married Woman
Ruling on the minimal gestation period for paternity attribution

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for establishing lineage (nasab) of a child born to a married woman and explain the effect of the husband's silence, acknowledgment, or denial.

Core Idea
A child born six months or more after marriage is attributed to the husband; denial requires li'an.

This passage establishes the key rule for lineage of a child from a married woman. The marital bed (firash) and the minimum gestation period of six months are central. The husband's response—silence, acknowledgment, or denial—affects the procedure but not the initial presumption. We will explore these rules and the role of li'an.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general ruling
State the condition under which a child born to a married woman is attributed to her husband.
Focus on the six-month minimum.
2
Analyze the exception
What happens if the child is born before six months? Why is the marriage considered corrupted?
Conception likely occurred before marriage.
3
Assess the husband's silence or acknowledgment
Determine the effect of the husband not speaking or confirming the child.
Both lead to lineage being established.
4
Examine the husband's denial
What happens if the husband denies paternity? How does li'an fit in?
Denial itself does not negate lineage; li'an is required.
5
Understand the role of women's testimony
Why is a woman's testimony sufficient to identify the child but not to establish lineage directly?
Lineage is based on the marital bed and duration, not testimony.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمنكوحة [بالجر أيضاً أي ويثبت نسب ولد المنكوحة. (ع)] أي يثبت نسب ولد المنكوحة إذا ولدت لستة أشهر فصاعداً أي أكثر منها، وانتصابه على الحال وذو الحال محذوف تقديره: فذهب صاعداً كما في قوله: اذهب راشداً، وإن جاءت به لأقل من ستة أشهر من يوم تزوجها لم يثبت نسبه؛ لان العلوق سابق على النكاح، فلا يكون منه، فيفسد النكاح لاحتمال أنه من زوج آخر بنكاح صحيح أو شبهة، وقوله: "وإن سكت" هكذا في أكثر النسخ، وفي بعضها: إن سكت بدون الواو الوصلية أي ثبوت نسب ولد المنكوحة للميعاد المتقدم إن سكت الزوج وكذا إن اعترف وقيد به للاحتراز عما لو نفاه كما سيأتي. (عيني، فتح) لستة أشهر : أي إذا ولدت لستة أشهر أو أكثر منها من وقت التزوج. (ع) وإن سكت: أي الزوج ولم يعترف به وهو واصل بما قبله؛ لأن الفراش قائم والمدة تامة وإن كان أقل منها لا يثبت. (ط،ع) وإن جحد إلخ [ أي الزوج الولادة حال قيام النكاح. (ط)] أي إن جحد الزوج الولادة في المسألة السابقة يثبت النسب بشهادة امرأة سواء كانت قابلة أو لا مقبولة الشهادة بأن كانت حرة مسلمة عدلة؛ لأن الفراش قائم والمدة تامة، فوجب القول بثبوته اعترف به الزوج أو سكت أو أنكر حتى لو نفى الزوج بعده يلاعن، ولا ينتفي إلا باللعان؛ لأنه ولد المنكوحة تبيين الحقائق : ٢٨٦/٣] فإن قيل: كيف يجب اللعان بنفي نسب ثبت بشهادة المرأة وهو حد على ما عرف، قلنا: النسب لا يثبت بشهادة المرأة، وإنما يثبت بها تعيين الولد ثم يثبت النسب بعد ذلك .
And the married woman [also in genitive, meaning the lineage of the child of the married woman is established. (Ayni)] meaning the lineage of the child of the married woman is established if she gives birth at six months or more, i.e., more than that, and it is in the accusative as a circumstantial adverb, the owner of the circumstance being omitted, estimated as 'so go upward', as in the verse 'go righteously'. If she brings the child at less than six months from the day of marriage, his lineage is not established because the conception preceded the marriage, so he is not from him, and the marriage is corrupted due to the possibility that the child is from another husband by a valid marriage or a doubtful act. His statement: 'and if he is silent' – this is in most copies, and in some: 'if he is silent' without the conjunctive waw – meaning the lineage of the child of the married woman is established for the aforementioned time if the husband is silent, and likewise if he acknowledges. It was specified to exclude the case of denial which will follow. (Ayni, Fath) Six months: i.e., if she gives birth at six months or more from the time of marriage. (Ayni) And if he is silent: i.e., the husband does not acknowledge, and it is connected to what precedes because the marital bed is present and the duration is complete. If it is less, it is not established. (Tawdih, Ayni) And if he denies, etc. [i.e., the husband denies the birth while the marriage is ongoing. (Tawdih)] i.e., if the husband denies the birth in the previous case, the lineage is established by the testimony of a woman, whether she is a midwife or not, provided her testimony is acceptable (free, Muslim, just), because the marital bed is present and the duration is complete, so it must be accepted that it is established, whether the husband acknowledges, remains silent, or denies. Even if the husband later repudiates him, he must perform li'an, and the child is not disowned except by li'an, because he is a child of the marriage bed. (Tabyin al-Haqaiq 3/286) If it is said: why is li'an necessary to deny a lineage that was established by the testimony of a woman? That is a punishment as known. We say: lineage is not established by the testimony of a woman; rather, it is only the identification of the child that is established, then lineage is established thereafter.
Source pages p. 516

Commentary
This passage deals with the child of a married woman (mankuhah). If she gives birth six months or more after the marriage, the child is attributed to the husband. If less than six months, the child is not attributed and the marriage is considered invalid (fasid) because conception occurred before marriage. The husband's silence or acknowledgment confirms the lineage; if he denies, the child is still initially attributed, and the husband must use li'an to deny paternity. The passage also clarifies that a woman's testimony can identify the child, but lineage itself is not established by that testimony alone; rather, it is the marital bed and the complete duration that establish lineage. Hence, if the husband denies, li'an is required because the child is presumed his.

Key Terms
منكوحة (mankuhah): A woman who is married in a valid marriage (nikah sahih).
لعان (li'an): Mutual cursing procedure used to deny paternity of a child born in marriage.

D) 2 years

Key Takeaway
The child's lineage is established only if born six months or more after marriage; denial requires li'an to negate.

Establishing Paternity for the Mu'taddah: Three Bases and an Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Explaining paternity establishment via pregnancy, acknowledgment, or heirs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three methods of establishing paternity for a mu'taddah, explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on the acceptance of her statement for the fact of birth, and apply the practical consequence in conditional talaq.

Core Idea
Paternity is established by apparent pregnancy, husband's acknowledgment, or heirs' confirmation; only the midwife identifies the child.

When a woman in iddah gives birth, her child's paternity must be established for inheritance, lineage, and related rulings. The Hanafi school outlines three bases for this: an obviously visible pregnancy (hubl zahir), the husband's own acknowledgment of the pregnancy (iqrar), or the confirmation of the deceased husband's heirs (tasdiq al-warithah). While there is consensus that the midwife's testimony is required to identify the specific child, a dispute arises between Abu Hanifa and his two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) on whether the woman's own statement about the fact of birth is sufficient. This lesson unpacks the three bases, the dispute, and its practical outcome.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three bases
From the matn and commentary, list the three scenarios that establish paternity: (1) apparent pregnancy (hubl zahir), (2) husband's acknowledgment (iqrar), (3) heirs' confirmation (tasdiq al-warithah). Note that the first two apply to divorce, the third to death.
The matn says: 'أو حبل ظاهر أو إقراره به أو تصديق الورثة'
2
Understand the role of the midwife
Recognize that regardless of the base, the midwife's testimony (shahadat al-qabibla) is required by consensus to identify the specific child (ta'yin al-walad). The woman's statement alone is insufficient for identification due to possible substitution.
The text states: 'لابد من شهادة القابلة لتعين الولد إجماعاً في جميع هذه الصور'
3
Locate the intra-Hanafi dispute
The dispute is over the fact of birth itself (wiladah). Abu Hanifa accepts the mu'taddah's statement in all three bases because she is a trustee (aminah) given the husband's acknowledgment or the apparent pregnancy. The Sahibayn require midwife testimony for the birth itself.
Dispute only appears in the establishment of birth, not paternity.
4
Analyze the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
The practical difference arises in conditional talaq or manumission linked to birth. For example, if a husband says to his wife in iddah, 'If you give birth, you are divorced,' Abu Hanifa would implement the divorce upon her word alone. The Sahibayn would not implement it until a midwife testifies.
The text explicitly mentions this as the fruit of the dispute.
5
Review the epistemological weight
The Zahir al-Riwayah position reflects the views of the three imams. The mufta bihi is not given in this passage, but the dispute is recognized within the school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب ثبوت النسب أو حبل ظاهر أو إقراره به أو تصديق الورثة والمنكوحة لستة أشهر فصاعداً وإن سكت، وإن جحد الزوج الحبل أو حبل ظاهر: أي يثبت نسب ولد المعتدة بشهادة القابلة مع وجود حبل ظاهر بالاتفاق، والمراد بظهور الحبل أن تكون أمارات حملها بالغة مبلغاً يوجب غلبة الظن كونها حاملاً لكل من شاهدها، وقوله: "أو إقراره به" أي يثبت أيضاً بإقرار من الزوج بالحبل؛ لأن النسب في هذين ثابت قبل الولادة. (فتح) أو تصديق الورثة : [ بالجر عطف على قوله بشهادة رجلين أي يثبت نسب ولد المعتدة عن وفات بتصديق الورثة كلهم أو بعضهم. (ع)] أي يثبت نسب ولد المعتدة عن وفات أن يصدق الورثة ما قالت المرأة ولم يشهدوا به، وهذا الثبوت في حق الإرث ظاهر؛ لأنه خالص حقهم، ويثبت في حق غيرهم أيضاً استحساناً، وإن كان القياس يأباه لما فيه من حمل النسب على الغير وهو الميت وجه الاستحسان أنهم قائمون مقام الميت فيقبل قولهم، ثم اعلم أنه لابد من شهادة القابلة لتعين الولد إجماعاً في جميع هذه الصور، والخلاف في ثبوت نفس الولادة بقول المعتدة، فعند أبي حنيفة: يثبت به في الصور الثلاث وعندهما لا يثبت إلا بشهادة القابلة وأما نسب الولد فلا يثبت بالإجماع إلا بشهادة القابلة؛ لاحتمال أن يكون هو غير هذا المعين، وثمرة الخلاف لا تظهر إلا في حق حكم آخر كالطلاق والعتاق بأن علقها بولادتها حتى يقع عند أبي حنيفة بقولها: ولدت؛ لأنها أمينة لاعترافه بالحمل أو لظهوره فيقبل قولها، وعندهما: لا يقع شيء حتى تشهد قابلة (فتح)
Chapter of Establishing Paternity. Or apparent pregnancy, or his acknowledgment of it, or the confirmation of the heirs, and the married woman (if she gives birth) after six months or more, and if he remains silent, and if the husband denies the pregnancy. 'Or apparent pregnancy': meaning the paternity of the child of the mu'taddah is established by the testimony of the midwife along with the presence of apparent pregnancy by consensus. The meaning of 'apparent pregnancy' is that the signs of her pregnancy have reached a level that makes anyone who sees her strongly believe she is pregnant. His statement: 'or his acknowledgment of it' means it is also established by the husband's acknowledgment of the pregnancy, because paternity in these two cases is confirmed before birth. 'Or the confirmation of the heirs': meaning the paternity of the child of the mu'taddah due to death is established by the confirmation of all or some of the heirs, i.e., the heirs confirm what the woman said without testifying to it. This establishment in terms of inheritance is apparent because it is purely their right; it is also established regarding others by istihsan, even though qiyas rejects it due to attributing paternity to another (the deceased). The basis of istihsan is that they stand in place of the deceased, so their statement is accepted. Then know that the testimony of the midwife is necessary to identify the child by consensus in all these cases. The dispute is regarding the establishment of the birth itself by the statement of the mu'taddah. According to Abu Hanifa: it is established in the three cases. According to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad): it is not established except by the midwife's testimony. As for the paternity of the child, it is not established by consensus except by the midwife's testimony, because it is possible that the child is not this specific one. The fruit of the dispute only appears in another ruling, like talaq and manumission, if he conditioned them on her giving birth: according to Abu Hanifa, they occur by her saying 'I gave birth' because she is trusted due to his acknowledgment of the pregnancy or its apparentness, so her statement is accepted. According to the two, nothing occurs until a midwife testifies.
Source pages p. 516

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq on establishing paternity for a woman in iddah (mu'taddah). The matn lists three ways: (1) an apparently visible pregnancy (hubl zahir), (2) the husband's own acknowledgment (iqrar) of the pregnancy, and (3) the confirmation of the heirs (tasdiq al-warithah) to the woman's claim. The commentary explains that in all three cases, the midwife's testimony is absolutely necessary to identify the specific child — this is by scholarly consensus. The woman's own statement about the fact of birth (wiladah) is disputed within the Hanafi school: Abu Hanifa accepts it in all three scenarios because she is effectively a trustee (aminah) once the husband has acknowledged the pregnancy or it is apparent; the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) require midwife testimony even for the birth itself. The practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf) emerges in conditional talaq or manumission linked to birth: Abu Hanifa would implement the condition upon her word alone, while the Sahibayn would not until a midwife testifies. The passage also notes that for paternity of the child itself (nasab al-walad), midwife testimony is always required by consensus to exclude the possibility of substitution.

Key Terms
معتدة (mu'taddah): A woman in the iddah period after divorce or death of her husband
قابلة (qabibla): A midwife who testifies to the delivery
حبل ظاهر (hubl zahir): An obviously visible pregnancy, where signs are clear to any observer
إقرار (iqrar): Acknowledgment, here the husband's admission of the pregnancy
تصديق الورثة (tasdiq al-warithah): Confirmation by the heirs of the deceased husband of the woman's claim
شهادة القابلة (shahadat al-qabibla): Testimony of a midwife to the birth and identity of the child

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is required to identify the specific child (ta'yin al-walad) in establishing paternity for a mu'taddah, regardless of the base?
A) The husband's acknowledgment
B) The mu'taddah's own statement
C) The midwife's testimony
D) The testimony of two male witnesses

Key Takeaway
Paternity for a mu'taddah is established through apparent pregnancy, husband's acknowledgment, or heirs' confirmation; while the midwife always identifies the child, Abu Hanifa accepts her statement for the birth itself, contrary to the Sahibayn.

Establishing Paternity: Six-Month Rule and Women's Testimony
Explaining paternity for a married woman with six-month minimum and husband's denial

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for establishing paternity in a married woman and apply the Hanafi ruling that one woman's testimony suffices to establish birth.

Core Idea
One woman's testimony establishes birth; husband's denial requires li'an, not rejection.

Paternity of a child born to a married woman is a foundational issue in family law. The Hanafi school presumes paternity from the marital bed (firash) once a minimum pregnancy duration is met, and the husband's denial does not automatically negate it. This lesson unpacks the conditions, the role of women's testimony, and the procedure of li'an.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling and its conditions
Read the passage to determine when a child's paternity is established for a married woman. Note the six-month minimum and the consequences if the period is less.
The passage uses 'بالجر أيضاً' indicating the married woman is in the genitive, but the ruling applies.
2
Analyze the husband's response: silence, acknowledgment, or denial
Observe that paternity is established if the husband is silent or acknowledges. If he denies, paternity is still established by the testimony of one woman.
The passage says 'يثبت النسب بشهادة امرأة' – paternity is established by a woman's testimony.
3
Understand the interplay of testimony and li'an
The passage addresses a question: how can li'an be required if paternity is already established by testimony? The answer distinguishes between establishing the child's identity and establishing paternity itself.
Li'an is a process to negate paternity; it is a penalty for denial after the child is attributed to the husband.
4
Examine the inter-madhhab dispute on number of witnesses
Compare the Hanafi position (one woman) with Shafi'i (four), Maliki (two), and Zufar (none). Understand the basis of each view.
The passage presents the Hanafi view as 'مذهبنا' and contrasts others.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمنكوحة [بالجر أيضاً أي ويثبت نسب ولد المنكوحة. (ع)] أي يثبت نسب ولد المنكوحة إذا ولدت لستة أشهر فصاعداً أي أكثر منها، وانتصابه على الحال وذو الحال محذوف تقديره: فذهب صاعداً كما في قوله: اذهب راشداً، وإن جاءت به لأقل من ستة أشهر من يوم تزوجها لم يثبت نسبه؛ لان العلوق سابق على النكاح، فلا يكون منه، فيفسد النكاح لاحتمال أنه من زوج آخر بنكاح صحيح أو شبهة، وقوله: "وإن سكت" هكذا في أكثر النسخ، وفي بعضها: إن سكت بدون الواو الوصلية أي ثبوت نسب ولد المنكوحة للميعاد المتقدم إن سكت الزوج وكذا إن اعترف وقيد به للاحتراز عما لو نفاه كما سيأتي. (ط،ع) وإن جحد إلخ [ أي الزوج الولادة حال قيام النكاح. (ط)] أي إن جحد الزوج الولادة في المسألة السابقة يثبت النسب بشهادة امرأة سواء كانت قابلة أو لا مقبولة الشهادة بأن كانت حرة مسلمة عدلة؛ لأن الفراش قائم والمدة تامة، فوجب القول بثبوته اعترف به الزوج أو سكت أو أنكر حتى لو نفى الزوج بعده يلاعن، ولا ينتفي إلا باللعان؛ لأنه ولد المنكوحة تبيين الحقائق : ٢٨٦/٣] فإن قيل: كيف يجب اللعان بنفي نسب ثبت بشهادة المرأة وهو حد على ما عرف، قلنا: النسب لا يثبت بشهادة المرأة، وإنما يثبت بها تعيين الولد ثم يثبت النسب بعد ذلك . فبشهادة امرأة على الولادة، فإن ولدت ثم اختلفا فقالت: نكحتني مذ ستة أشهر وادعى الأقل، فالقول لها، وهو ابنه، ولو علق طلاقها بولادتها، وشهدت امرأة على الولادة لم تطلق، وإن كان أقرّ بالحبل طلّقت بلا شهادة وأكثر مدة الحمل سنتان، عندنا من وقت التزوج وعند بالفراش ضرورة كونه مولوداً في فراشه ثم ثبوت النسب بشهادة امرأة على الولادة هو مذهبنا، الشافعي: يثبت بشهادة أربع نسوة، وعند مالك بشهادة امرأتين، وعند زفر لا يثبت بشهادة النساء. (فتح) فبشهادة امرأة أي فيثبت بشهادة امرأة مسلمة مقبولة الشهادة تشهد على الولادة. (ط،ع) على الولادة: حتى لو نفاه الزوج لاعن به وهذا بالإجماع. (ع) فالقول لها وهو ابنه: [ أي ابن الزوج بشهادة الظاهر. (ع)] لأن الظاهر يشهد لها، فإنها تلد ظاهراً من نكاح لا من سفاح، فإن قيل: الظاهر يشهد له أيضاً؛ لأن الحوادث تضاف إلى أقرب الأوقات، والنكاح حادث، قلنا: النسب مما يحتال لإثباته احتياطاً إحياء للولد، ثم هل تستحلف المرأة أم لا فهو على الاختلاف المشهور عند أبي حنيفة وصاحبيه في الأشياء الستة، والنسب والنكاح من الستة، والفتوى على قولهما أنها تحلف (مستخلص، فتح)
And the married woman: meaning the paternity of the child of the married woman is established if she gives birth after six months or more. The accusative is a circumstantial accusative with the implied subject 'it goes increasing' as in 'go rightly'. If she gives birth in less than six months from the day of marriage, paternity is not established because conception preceded the marriage, so he is not the father; the marriage is invalidated due to the possibility that the child is from another husband in a valid marriage or [from] doubtful intercourse. His statement: 'and if he is silent' – in most copies it is thus; in some copies it is 'if he is silent' without the conjunctive waw, meaning the paternity of the married woman's child is established for the aforementioned period if the husband is silent, and similarly if he acknowledges; it is restricted to guard against the case where he denies it, as will come. 'For six months': meaning if she gives birth at six months or more from the time of marriage. 'And if he is silent': meaning the husband does not acknowledge, and it is connected to what precedes because the marital bed (firash) is established and the period is complete; if it was less than that, it is not established. 'And if he denies etc.': meaning if the husband denies the birth while the marriage is ongoing, paternity is established by the testimony of a woman, whether she is a midwife or not, provided she is acceptable: free, Muslim, and just. Because the marital bed is established and the period is complete, so it must be ruled that paternity is established, whether the husband acknowledges, is silent, or denies. Even if the husband denies afterwards, he must perform li'an, and it is not negated except by li'an because the child is of the married woman. If it is said: How can li'an be required for denying paternity that is established by a woman's testimony, when li'an is a penalty as known? We say: Paternity is not established by a woman's testimony; rather, by it the identity of the child is established, then paternity is established after that. So by the testimony of one woman over the birth: if she gives birth then they dispute, and she says 'he married me six months ago' and he claims less, her statement is accepted, and the child is his. And if he conditioned her talaq on her giving birth, and one woman testified over the birth, she is not divorced; but if he had acknowledged the pregnancy, she is divorced without testimony. And the maximum duration of pregnancy is two years according to us, from the time of marriage. And according to... due to the marital bed being necessary, as the child is born in his bed; then establishing paternity by the testimony of one woman over the birth is our madhhab. Al-Shafi'i: it is established by the testimony of four women; according to Malik, by the testimony of two women; according to Zufar, it is not established by the testimony of women. 'By the testimony of one woman': meaning it is established by the testimony of one Muslim woman whose testimony is acceptable, witnessing the birth. 'Over the birth': even if the husband denies it, he must li'an by it, and this is by consensus. 'Her statement is accepted and the child is his': because the apparent evidence (zahir) supports her, as she gives birth apparently from a marriage, not from fornication. If it is said: The apparent also supports him, because events are attributed to the nearest time, and the marriage is a recent event. We say: Paternity is something to be established cautiously to preserve the child's life. Then, whether the woman is to be sworn or not, there is the well-known difference between Abu Hanifa and his two companions regarding the six matters; paternity and marriage are among the six. The fatwa is according to their opinion that she is sworn.
Source pages p. 516

Commentary
This passage deals with paternity for a married woman (mankuhah). The ruling states: if she gives birth at least six months after the marriage, paternity is attributed to the husband. If she gives birth in less than six months, paternity is not established because conception must have occurred before marriage — this also invalidates the marriage due to possible zina or a prior valid marriage. The husband's silence or acknowledgment both lead to paternity; if he denies, paternity is still established by the testimony of a single acceptable woman (free, Muslim, just) testifying to the birth. However, if the husband persists in denial, he must perform li'an (mutual cursing) to disown the child; paternity cannot be negated otherwise. The passage addresses a query: how can li'an be required for denying something established by a woman's testimony? The answer: the testimony establishes the identity of the child, not paternity per se; paternity follows from the child being born on the marital bed. If the wife and husband dispute the time of marriage — she claims it was six months ago, he claims less — her statement is accepted, and the child is his. The Hanafi school's position is that one woman's testimony suffices (as opposed to Shafi'i requiring four, Malik requiring two, and Zufar rejecting women's testimony entirely). There is also a note on whether the wife must take an oath; the famous difference between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on the six matters (including paternity and marriage) is referenced, and the fatwa follows the Sahibayn that she must swear.

Key Terms
فراش (firash): The marital bed; the legal presumption that a child born in wedlock is attributed to the husband
لعان (li'an): Mutual cursing between spouses when husband denies paternity; results in dissolution of marriage and negation of paternity

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, how many women's testimonies are sufficient to establish paternity for a married woman when the husband denies the birth?
A) Two women
B) Four women
C) One woman (free, Muslim, just)
D) One man

Key Takeaway
One woman's testimony suffices to establish paternity in a married woman's birth; the husband's denial requires li'an, not rejection of the testimony.

Conditional Talaq: One Woman's Testimony and the Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on testimony and conditional talaq

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on talaq conditioned on birth with one woman's testimony, and explain the legal reasoning (qiyas and hadith) behind each view.

Core Idea
A single woman's testimony suffices for birth, but not for talaq according to Abu Hanifa.

This lesson examines a classic intra-Hanafi dispute about conditional talaq. When a husband says, 'If you give birth, you are divorced,' and one woman testifies to the birth, do we accept that testimony as proof of talaq? Abu Hanifa and his two main disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn), differ on this question. Their disagreement hinges on whether the testimony that is accepted for the birth should automatically be accepted for its legal consequence—the talaq. We will explore the proofs cited by each side and understand the practical implications of this disagreement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core case
Read the passage carefully: a husband says 'if you give birth, you are divorced.' A single woman (not the wife) testifies that she gave birth. Does talaq occur?
This is the scenario at the heart of the dispute.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and proof
Abu Hanifa holds that talaq does not occur. Understand his reasoning: the wife is claiming a breach (hanth), which requires complete proof (hujjah tammah). The testimony of one woman is accepted only out of necessity (for matters men cannot see), but talaq is not a necessary consequence of birth.
Note his analogy: buying meat and a single witness says it is from a Magian slaughter; the testimony is accepted to forbid eating, but not to claim restitution from the seller.
3
State the Sahibayn's position and proof
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that talaq does occur. Their proof: the hadith 'The testimony of women is permissible in matters which men cannot see' (delivery being such a matter). Since the testimony is accepted for the birth, it should also be accepted for what is based upon it, i.e., talaq.
Also note that if the husband acknowledges the pregnancy, Abu Hanifa accepts the wife's word alone for talaq, but the Sahibayn still require midwife testimony.
4
Analyze the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
Consider what changes in real life: under Abu Hanifa, the woman is not considered divorced until she produces stronger evidence (e.g., two women or one woman and two men? actually two women or one woman and oath? the text says 'complete proof' — likely two women or one woman and one man). Under the Sahibayn, she is divorced immediately upon the single woman's testimony.
This directly affects her remarriage, waiting period, and rights.
5
Evaluate the strength of each proof
Compare the two proofs: Abu Hanifa uses qiyas (analogy) from meat buying to show that testimony of one is not universally valid for all legal consequences. The Sahibayn rely on a hadith and the principle that birth and talaq are inseparable here. Which reasoning is more sound? The passage does not give a tarjih, so you must weigh the arguments.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf within the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو علق طلاقها بولادتها، وشهدت امرأة على الولادة لم تطلق، وإن كان أقرّ بالحبل طلّقت بلا شهادة لم تطلق : [ أي لم تقبل الشهادة و لم تطلق المرأة؛ لأنها ادعت الحنث فلا يثبت إلا بحجة تامة. (ع)] عند أبي حنيفة أي من قال لامرأته: إن ولدت فأنت طالق، فشهدت امرأة على الولادة لا يقع الطلاق عنده؛ لأنها ادعت الحنث، فلا يثبت إلا بحجة تامة؛ لان قبول شهادتهن ضرورية فلا تظهر في حق الطلاق؛ لأنه ليس من ضرورات الولادة؛ إذ الطلاق ينفك عن الولادة في الجملة، وإن صار عن لوازمها هنا باتفاق الحال كمن اشترى لحماً فأخبره عدل أنه ذبيحة المجوسي، قبلت شهادته في حق حرمة الأكل، ولا يثبت كون الذابح بجوسياً بشهادة الواحد في حق الرجوع على البائع، وقالا: تطلق؛ لأن شهادتها حجة في ذلك؛ لقوله عليه: "شهادة النساء جائزة فيما لا يستطيع الرجال النظر إليه"؛ ولأنها لما قبلت في الولادة تقبل فيما يبتنى عليهما وهو الطلاق. (مستخلص، فتح) بالحبل: فيما إذا علق طلاقها بالولادة أو كان الحبل ظاهراً. (ع) طلقت أي تطلق بقولها: ولدت من غير شهادة أحد. (ع) بلا شهادة يعني تطلق بقولها: ولدت من غير شهادة أحد. (ع)] هذا عند أبي حنيفة؛ لأن الإقرار بالحبل إقرار بما يفضي إليه وهو الولادة؛ لأنه أقر بكونها مؤتمنة فيقبل قولها في رد الأمانة، وقالا: تشترط شهادة القابلة؛ لأنها تدعي الحنث، فلا يقبل قولها بدون الحجة، وشهادة القابلة حجة في مثله، وأما النسب ولوازمه كأمومية الولد فلا يثبت بدون شهادة القابلة اتفاقاً، وعلى هذا الخلاف لو كان الحبل ظاهراً. (عيني، فتح)
And if he conditioned her talaq on her giving birth, and a woman testified over the birth, she is not divorced; but if he had acknowledged the pregnancy, she is divorced without testimony. 'Not divorced': meaning the testimony is not accepted and the woman is not divorced because she is claiming a breach (hanth), and that is not established except by complete proof. According to Abu Hanifa, i.e., someone who said to his wife 'if you give birth, you are divorced', and a woman testified over the birth, talaq does not occur in his view because she is claiming a breach, and that is not established except by complete proof. Since the acceptance of their testimony is necessary/permissible (dharuriyyah), it does not appear in the matter of talaq because it is not among the necessities of birth; although talaq is disconnected from birth in general, even if it becomes among its necessary consequences here due to the agreement of the situation. It is like someone who bought meat and a just person informed him that it is the slaughter of a Magian: his testimony is accepted regarding the prohibition of eating it, but it is not established that the slaughterer is a Magian by the testimony of one person regarding recourse against the seller. And the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: she is divorced because her testimony is a proof in that matter due to his statement (peace be upon him): 'The testimony of women is permissible in matters which men cannot see'; and because when it is accepted regarding the birth, it is accepted regarding what is based upon it, which is talaq. 'By the pregnancy': in the case where he conditioned her talaq on birth or the pregnancy was apparent. 'She is divorced': meaning she is divorced by her saying 'I gave birth' without anyone's testimony. 'Without testimony': meaning she is divorced by her saying 'I gave birth' without anyone's testimony. This is according to Abu Hanifa because acknowledging the pregnancy is an acknowledgment of what leads to it, i.e., birth; because he acknowledged that she is trustworthy, so her statement is accepted in returning the trust. And the two said: the testimony of the midwife is required because she is claiming breach, so her statement is not accepted without proof, and the midwife's testimony is proof in such cases. As for paternity and its consequences like the motherhood of the child, it is not established without the midwife's testimony by agreement. And upon this difference, if the pregnancy was apparent.
Source pages p. 517

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute on the case where a husband makes talaq conditional on his wife's delivery (e.g., 'If you give birth, you are divorced'). If a single woman (not necessarily a midwife) testifies that she gave birth, does talaq occur? Abu Hanifa holds that it does not, because the wife is claiming that the condition (hanth) has occurred, and such a claim requires complete proof (hujjah tammah). The testimony of one woman, while accepted in matters of birth itself, is not sufficient to establish a breach that leads to talaq, as talaq is not a necessary consequence of birth in all cases. He gives an analogy: if one buys meat and a trustworthy person says it is from a Magian slaughter, that testimony is accepted to forbid eating, but not to establish that the seller sold Magian meat for restitution. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) argue that talaq does occur, because her testimony is a valid proof in matters where men cannot see (citing the hadith), and since it is accepted for the birth itself, it should be accepted for the legal consequence (talaq) that is based upon it. However, if the husband had previously acknowledged the pregnancy (iqrar bi al-habl), then according to Abu Hanifa, the woman's word alone suffices to trigger talaq without any testimony, because her acknowledgment of pregnancy implies trust in her statement. The Sahibayn still require midwife testimony even in that case. For paternity and related matters (like the child's maternity), midwife testimony is required by agreement.

Key Terms
حنث (hanth): Breach of a conditional oath; here, the occurrence of the condition (birth) that triggers talaq
علق طلاقها (allaqa talaqaha): To make talaq conditional upon a future event, here birth

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The testimony of a single woman is never accepted in any legal matter.
B) The woman claiming breach (hanth) requires complete proof, and one woman's testimony is not complete proof.
C) The husband must acknowledge the birth first.
D) The testimony must come from the wife herself.

Key Takeaway
Within the Hanafi school, there is a legitimate disagreement over whether a single woman's testimony suffices to trigger a conditional talaq on birth: Abu Hanifa requires stronger proof, while the Sahibayn accept it.

Maximum Duration of Pregnancy: Hanafi Position and Inter-Madhhab Differences
Listing maximum pregnancy durations according to different schools

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the maximum duration of pregnancy, list the positions of other major schools, and explain the practical implication (thamarah al-khilaf) for paternity.

Core Idea
The Hanafi maximum pregnancy duration of two years is a legal presumption, not a medical fact, and has direct impact on paternity.

This lesson presents a concise listing of maximum pregnancy durations across different schools. The Hanafi school holds two years, while other schools extend to four, five, or even seven years. This dispute is not merely theoretical—it directly determines whether a child is legally attributed to the husband. Understanding these differences is essential for judges and muftis dealing with paternity cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi position
Locate the statement marked by 'عندنا' (according to us) in the Arabic passage. It states the maximum is two years.
The term 'عندنا' signals the author's madhhab (Hanafi).
2
List the positions of other schools
Read the passage sequentially and note each school or authority mentioned: Shafi'i (4 years), Maliki and Hanbali (5 years, with a 7-year narration), al-Zuhri (6 years), al-Layth (3 years).
Some positions are reported as multiple narrations from the same imam.
3
Recognize the thamarah al-khilaf
Consider the practical consequence: if a child is born after the Hanafi maximum of two years, the Hanafi judge does not attribute the child to the husband. Under other schools, attribution may be possible up to 4 or 5 years.
This affects inheritance, lineage, and custody.
4
Understand the basis of the difference
Note that these are legal presumptions based on transmitted reports from early scholars, not on medical observation. The Hanafi view relies on reports from prominent Companions and Successors.
This explains why the difference persists despite modern medical knowledge.

Commentary
This passage is a concise enumeration of the maximum legal duration of pregnancy according to various schools and early authorities. The Hanafi position (عندنا) is two years. Al-Shafi'i held four years. The Maliki and Hanbali schools are reported with five years as the famous view, with a narration of seven years from Ahmad (attributed also to Rabi'ah). Al-Zuhri said six years, and al-Layth ibn Sa'd said three years. This dispute has practical implications for establishing paternity: if a woman gives birth after more than two years from the last possible contact with her husband, a Hanafi judge would not attribute the child to him, while a Shafi'i judge might do so up to four years, etc. The passage does not provide the evidence for each school's position, but simply records the differences.

سنتان (sanatan): Two years (the Hanafi maximum)

Single Woman's Testimony: Lineage vs. Conditional Divorce
Ruling on lineage proof and conditional divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding whether a single woman's testimony establishes conditional divorce, and state the mufta bihi ruling.

Core Idea
Proof of birth and proof of divorce are separate: a single woman's testimony proves the first, not necessarily the second.

This lesson examines a nuanced dispute within the Hanafi school: when a man suspends divorce on childbirth, does a single woman's testimony proving the birth also trigger the divorce? Abu Hanifa says no, while his two disciples say yes. The ruling has implications for how evidence is weighed in matters of lineage and family law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the common scenario
A husband says to his wife, 'You are divorced if you give birth.' A single woman testifies to the birth. Does the divorce occur?
This is not about lineage; that is uncontested.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and proof
Abu Hanifa holds that the divorce does not occur. His proof: women's testimony is a concession limited to matters men cannot see; divorce is not a necessary consequence of birth, so stronger proof is needed. He analogizes to a meat buyer who accepts one witness for permissibility but not for recourse against the seller.
3
State the Sahibayn's position and proof
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that the divorce does occur. Their proof: the hadith 'The testimony of women is permissible in matters men cannot see' covers both birth and its legal effects. Since the testimony is accepted for the birth, it should be accepted for the dependent ruling (divorce).
4
Analyze the key difference
The disagreement hinges on whether the acceptance of testimony is limited to the specific fact (birth) or extends to its legal consequences. Abu Hanifa sees it as a restricted concession; the Sahibayn see it as a general proof for all related rulings.
Also note the related dispute on acknowledged pregnancy: Abu Hanifa accepts the wife's statement alone; the Sahibayn require a midwife's testimony.
5
Identify the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position
The text states 'al-fatwa `ala qawlihima' – the fatwa is on the view of the Sahibayn. In practice, the wife is to be sworn in cases of lineage and marriage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(ع) لم تطلق : [ أي لم تقبل الشهادة و لم تطلق المرأة؛ لأنها ادعت الحنث فلا يثبت إلا بحجة تامة. (ع)] عند أبي حنيفة أي من قال لامرأته: إن ولدت فأنت طالق، فشهدت امرأة على الولادة لا يقع الطلاق عنده؛ لأنها ادعت الحنث، فلا يثبت إلا بحجة تامة؛ لان قبول شهادتهن ضرورية فلا تظهر في حق الطلاق؛ لأنه ليس من ضرورات الولادة؛ إذ الطلاق ينفك عن الولادة في الجملة، وإن صار عن لوازمها هنا باتفاق الحال كمن اشترى لحماً فأخبره عدل أنه ذبيحة المجوسي، قبلت شهادته في حق حرمة الأكل، ولا يثبت كون الذابح بجوسياً بشهادة الواحد في حق الرجوع على البائع، وقالا: تطلق؛ لأن شهادتها حجة في ذلك؛ لقوله عليه: "شهادة النساء جائزة فيما لا يستطيع الرجال النظر إليه"؛ ولأنها لما قبلت في الولادة تقبل فيما يبتنى عليهما وهو الطلاق. (ع)] هذا عند أبي حنيفة؛ لأن الإقرار بالحبل إقرار بما يفضي إليه وهو الولادة؛ لأنه أقر بكونها مؤتمنة فيقبل قولها في رد الأمانة، وقالا: تشترط شهادة القابلة؛ لأنها تدعي الحنث، فلا يقبل قولها بدون الحجة، وشهادة القابلة حجة في مثله، وأما النسب ولوازمه كأمومية الولد فلا يثبت بدون شهادة القابلة اتفاقاً، وعلى هذا الخلاف لو كان الحبل ظاهراً. (عيني، فتح)
Book of Divorce: Chapter of Establishing Lineage. By the testimony of one woman on childbirth, then if she gave birth and they disagreed and she said: 'He married me six months ago' and he claimed the lesser, the statement is hers, and he is his son. And if he suspended her divorce upon her childbirth, and one woman testified on the husband... she is not divorced. And if he acknowledged pregnancy, she is divorced without testimony. And the maximum period of pregnancy is two years according to us. ...
Source pages p. 517

Commentary
This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq establishes the Hanafi ruling on proving lineage through a single woman's testimony regarding childbirth. It begins by stating that if a woman gives birth and the husband disputes the timeline, her word is accepted, and the child is attributed to him. The text then addresses a conditional divorce: if a man says 'You are divorced if you give birth,' and a single woman testifies to the birth, Abu Hanifa holds that the divorce does not occur because the woman is claiming the breach (al-hinth), which requires stronger proof. The acceptance of women's testimony is a concession limited to matters men cannot see, so it does not extend to divorce, which is not a necessary consequence of birth. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (al-Shaybani) disagree, arguing that the testimony is accepted for the birth and therefore for its legal effect (divorce), based on the hadith 'The testimony of women is permissible in matters men cannot see.' The text further discusses the case of acknowledged pregnancy: Abu Hanifa rules that if the husband acknowledges the pregnancy, the wife's mere statement of birth suffices for divorce, as her trustworthiness is established; the Sahibayn require a midwife's testimony. The relied-upon fatwa (mufta bihi) is that the wife is to be sworn in cases of lineage and marriage, following the position of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad.

Key Terms
ثبوت النسب (thubut al-nasab): Legal establishment of a child's lineage to the father.
حنث (hanth): The occurrence of the conditional event (here, childbirth) that triggers a divorce or oath.
قابلة (qabalah): Midwife who attends childbirth; her testimony is required by Sahibayn in cases of acknowledged pregnancy.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school accepts a single woman's testimony for lineage, but its use for conditional divorce was contested; the operative ruling is that it suffices after the wife is sworn.
