Ranking of Female Relatives in Custody
Explains the hierarchy of female relatives entitled to custody

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi hierarchy of female relatives entitled to custody and explain the rationales behind the preferences (e.g., agnatic tie over blood proximity), including the inter-madhhab dispute regarding the sister for the father vs. sister for the mother.

Core Idea
In Hanafi law, custody follows a detailed hierarchy where agnatic connection through the father takes precedence over blood proximity through the mother.

The Hanafi school has detailed rules for which female relative is most entitled to custody when the mother is unavailable. This passage from Kanz al-Daqa’iq and its commentary presents the ranking, starting from the mother and grandmother, then moving to sisters, aunts, and more distant relations. It also highlights a key disagreement with Imam al-Shafi’i and Imam Ahmad over whether the sister for the father or the sister for the mother should be preferred. Understanding this hierarchy is essential for a qadi or mufti when adjudicating custody cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base ordering
Begin by listing the first four categories in order: mother, grandmother, father, then maternal aunts and paternal aunts. Note that the mother and grandmother are the most entitled.
The text says: 'الأم والجدة أحق به' – mother and grandmother are most entitled.
2
Understand the ranking among sisters
Next, focus on the sisters. The text declares that after the sister for the mother, the sister for the father is more entitled. This is the Hanafi position. Then examine the disputes: al-Shafi’i and Ahmad prefer the sister for the mother due to closer lineage; Zufar says they share equally.
The Hanafi reliance on agnatic connection (asabah) is the key principle here.
3
Analyze the role of maternal aunts and the hadith
The passage mentions a narration that the maternal aunt is preferred over the sister for the father, citing the hadith 'The maternal aunt is like a mother.' However, the relied-upon Hanafi view gives the maternal aunt a lower rank than the sister for the father. Note the nuance with the daughters of the sister.
The hadith is not strong enough to override the general principle of asabah in Hanafi usul.
4
Follow the fuller hierarchy for aunts and beyond
The commentary extends the hierarchy to types of maternal and paternal aunts (full, consanguine, uterine) and then to the aunts of the mother and father. The last category before exclusion is the maternal aunt of the mother, then maternal aunts of the father, etc.
The pattern is: full sister > uterine sister > consanguine sister; similarly for aunts.
5
Identify who is excluded
The passage ends by stating that daughters of uncles and aunts (i.e., female cousins) have no right to custody. This is because they are not mahram to the child, and their kinship is not confirmed by the required relationship.
This exclusion is based on the condition that the female custodian must be a mahram to the child.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الحضانة لأب، ثم الخالات كذلك، ثم العمّاتُ كذلك. والأم والجدّة أحق به. ثم لأب: أي بعد الأخت لأم، الأخت لأب أحق بالولد، وعند الشافعي في الجديد وأحمد: هي أحق الأخت من لأم لقرب القرابة، وعند زفر : هما تشتركان؛ لأنهما تستويان في الإدلاء بالأم، وفي رواية: الخالة تقدم على الأخت لأب؛ لقوله عليه: "الخالة والدة"، وبنات الأخت لأب وأم أو لأم أولى من الخالات، واختلفت الروايات في بنات الأخت لأب والأصح أن الخالة أولى منهن وبنات الأخت أولى من بنات الأخ، وإذا اجتمع من له حق الحضانة في درجة فأورعهم أولى ثم أكبرهم. ثم الخالاتُ كذلك أي بعد المذكورات الخالات ينزلن كالأخوات فمن كانت لأب وأم فهي أولى ثم لأم ثم لأب. ثم العمات كذلك: أي بعد الخالات أحق بالحضانة العمات كالترتيب المذكور. ومن كانت لأب وأم فهي أولى ثم لأم ثم لأب، وبعدهن خالة الأم الشقيقة، ثم لأم ثم لأب، ثم عماتها كذلك، وخالة الأم أولى خالة الأب، ثم خالات الأب وعماته كذلك، وأما بنات الأعمام والعمات والأخوال والخالات فلا حق من لهن في الحضانة؛ لأن قرابتهن لم تتأكد بالمحرمية.
Chapter of Custody: The father [is entitled], then the maternal aunts likewise, then the paternal aunts likewise. And the mother and grandmother are more entitled to him. Then for the father: meaning after the sister for the mother, the sister for the father is more entitled to the child. According to al-Shafi'i in the new [opinion] and Ahmad: she is more entitled than the sister for the mother due to closeness of kinship. According to Zufar: they share [equally] because they are equal in tracing through the mother. And in one narration: the maternal aunt is preferred over the sister for the father due to his statement: 'The maternal aunt is like a mother.' And the daughters of the sister for father and mother or for mother are more entitled than maternal aunts. And the narrations differ concerning the daughters of the sister for the father, and the correct [view] is that the maternal aunt is more entitled than them, and the daughters of the sister are more entitled than the daughters of the brother. And when those who have the right to custody gather at a level, then the most pious is most entitled, then the eldest. Then the maternal aunts likewise: i.e., after the aforementioned, the maternal aunts descend like sisters: so she who is for father and mother is most entitled, then for mother, then for father. Then the paternal aunts likewise: i.e., after the maternal aunts, the paternal aunts are more entitled to custody in the mentioned order. And she who is for father and mother is most entitled, then for mother, then for father. And after them, the maternal aunt of the mother, full sister, then for mother, then for father, then her paternal aunts likewise. And the maternal aunt of the mother is more entitled than the maternal aunt of the father, then the maternal aunts of the father and his paternal aunts likewise. As for the daughters of paternal uncles, paternal aunts, maternal uncles, and maternal aunts, they have no right to custody because their kinship is not confirmed by mahram relationship.
Source pages p. 521

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq on the chapter of custody, followed by a commentary (sharh) that expands and provides proofs and disputes. The base ruling establishes the order: after the mother and grandmother, the father, maternal aunts, and paternal aunts are entitled. The commentary then details the hierarchy among sisters and aunts, and includes inter-madhhab disputes. According to the Hanafi school, the sister for the father is preferred over the sister for the mother, whereas al-Shafi'i and Ahmad prefer the sister for the mother due to closer lineage. Zufar holds they share equally. There is also a narration that the maternal aunt is preferred over the sister for the father, citing the hadith 'The maternal aunt is like a mother.' The correct Hanafi position is that the sister for the father is more entitled, but the maternal aunt is more entitled than the daughter of the sister for the father. The commentary further ranks the various types of maternal and paternal aunts, and clarifies that daughters of uncles and aunts have no custody because they are not mahram to the child. This illustrates the detailed legal reasoning (istidlal) behind the Hanafi rules of female custody.

Key Terms
حضانة (hadānah): The legal right and responsibility of caring for a minor child, including physical custody and upbringing.
محرم (mahram): A person with whom marriage is permanently prohibited due to blood relationship or fosterage.
عصبات (ʿaṣabāt): Agnatic male relatives who inherit as residuaries; they are also entitled to custody after female relatives.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, who is more entitled to custody of a child: the sister for the father (أخت لأب) or the sister for the mother (أخت لأم)?
A) The sister for the mother is more entitled.
B) The sister for the father is more entitled.
C) They are equally entitled.
D) The maternal aunt is more entitled than both.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi custody hierarchy prioritizes agnatic ties among female relatives, so a sister for the father outranks a sister for the mother, and maternal aunts outrank paternal aunts after sisters.

Effect of Marriage to Non-Mahram on Custody
Explains that a woman loses custody if she marries a non-mahram of the child

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on when a custodial woman loses her right upon marriage, identify which marriages preserve her right, and explain the condition for regaining custody.

Core Idea
A custodial woman loses her right only if she marries a non-mahram of the child, not if the husband is a close relative.

In this passage, we examine a key condition of custody in Hanafi fiqh: a woman’s right to care for a child can be suspended if she marries a man who is not a mahram of the child. The ruling is based on a prophetic hadith and the principle of avoiding harm. We will unpack the ruling, its proof, and the circumstances under which custody is regained.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its condition
The text states that any woman entitled to custody loses that right if she marries someone who is not a mahram of the child. The ruling is conditioned on the husband being a non-mahram.
The default assumption is that marriage can affect custody; the Hanafi school specifies the type.
2
Locate the dalil
The author cites the hadith: 'You are more entitled to him as long as you do not marry.' This is the primary evidence.
The hadith is authentic and serves as the proof for the ruling.
3
Analyze the exception
Marriage to a mahram of the child does not cause loss of custody. Examples: grandmother marries grandfather, mother marries child's paternal uncle, etc. The rationale is that such a husband will have compassion (shafaqah).
The term 'mahram' here includes blood, fosterage, and marriage relationships.
4
Determine the return of the right
When the marriage ends (by death, final divorce, or annulment), the woman regains custody. However, if the divorce is revocable (raj‘i), she must wait until the ‘iddah expires because the marriage tie persists.
This is analogous to the nashizah (rebellious wife) case and other instances of removed guardianship.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سقط حقها [ أي في الحضانة لوجود الضرر حينئذ من جهة زوج الأم . (ع)] أي كل امرأة من هؤلاء اللاتي لهن حق في الحضانة إذا تزوجت بغير محرم الصغير سقط حقها في الحضانة؛ لقوله علية: "أنت أحق به ما لم تتزوجي"، وقيد بغير المحرم؛ لعدم الشفقة فإنه ينفق على الولد قليلاً وينظر إليه مبغضاً، بخلاف ما إذا كان الزوج ذا للصغير كالجدة إذا كان زوجها الجد، أو الأم إذا كان زوجها عم الصغير، أو الخالة إذا كان زوجها عمه أو محرم أخاه، أو العمة إذا كان زوجها خاله أو أخاه من أمه لا يسقط حقها؛ لانتفاء الضرر عن الصغير لقيام الشفقة. (مستخلص، عيني) ثم يعود بالفرقة : [ بعد ما سقط بالتزوج لزوال المانع. (ع)] أي من سقط حقها بالتزوج يعود حقها إذا ارتفعت الزوجية؛ لأن المانع قد زال كالناشزة يسقط حقها، ثم إذا عادت إلى منزل الزوج تجب النفقة، وكذا الولاية تزول بالجنون والارتداد، ثم إذا زال ذلك عادت الولاية، ثم إذا كان الطلاق رجعياً لا يعود حقها حتى تنقضي عدتها لقيام الزوجية. (فتح، مستخلص، عيني)
Her right falls [i.e., in custody due to the existence of harm at that time from the stepfather's side]. Meaning: any woman from among those who have a right in custody, if she marries someone who is not a mahram of the child, her right in custody falls due to his statement: 'You are more entitled to him as long as you do not marry.' And it is restricted to non-mahram because of lack of compassion, for he spends little on the child and looks at him with hatred. Contrary to the case where the husband is a relative of the child, like if the grandmother's husband is the grandfather, or the mother's husband is the child's paternal uncle, or the maternal aunt's husband is her paternal uncle or mahram brother, or the paternal aunt's husband is her maternal uncle or brother from her mother – her right does not fall due to absence of harm upon the child because compassion exists. Then it returns with separation: [after it fell due to marriage, due to the removal of the barrier]. Meaning: she whose right fell due to marriage, her right returns when the marriage ceases because the barrier has been removed. Just like the nashizah (rebellious wife) loses her right [to nafaqah], then when she returns to the husband's house, nafaqah becomes obligatory. Similarly, guardianship is removed by insanity and apostasy, then when that is removed, guardianship returns. And if the divorce is revocable (raj'i), her right does not return until her 'iddah expires because the marriage tie remains.
Source pages p. 521

Commentary
This passage is an expansion (sharh) on the ruling that a woman entitled to custody loses that right if she marries a man who is not a mahram of the child. The proof is the hadith: 'You are more entitled to him as long as you do not marry.' The rationale is that a stepfather who is not a close relative will lack natural affection, may mistreat the child, and harm is expected. However, if the husband is a mahram of the child – e.g., the grandmother marries the grandfather, the mother marries the child's paternal uncle, etc. – then custody is not lost because the husband is likely to show compassion. The passage then clarifies that if the marriage ends (by death, divorce, etc.), the woman regains custody because the obstacle has been removed, just like a nashizah (rebellious wife) regains nafaqah when she returns to obedience. An important nuance: if the divorce is revocable (raj'i), custody does not return until the 'iddah expires because the marriage is still in effect. This is based on qiyas with similar cases in family law.

Key Terms
محرم (mahram): A relative within the prohibited degrees of marriage; a husband who is a mahram of the child means he is so closely related that marriage would be permanently prohibited.
فرقة (furqah): Separation of husband and wife (by death, divorce, or annulment), after which the obstacle to custody is removed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When does a woman who lost custody due to marriage regain her right?
A) Immediately upon divorce, even if revocable.
B) Only after she remarries another man.
C) When the marriage ends (by death or final divorce), and if revocable, after 'iddah.
D) She never regains it once lost.

Key Takeaway
A custodial woman loses her right only when she marries a non-mahram of the child, and regains it once the marriage ends (fully or after 'iddah if revocable).

Custody by Male Relatives: Order and Conditions
Outlines the order of male agnatic relatives entitled to custody

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the order of male agnatic relatives entitled to custody and apply the conditions of mahram and trustworthiness.

Core Idea
Custody among male agnates follows inheritance order: closest first, with piety and mahram restrictions for girls.

The Hanafi school provides a clear hierarchy for male custody when no qualified female relative is available. This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq enumerates the order of 'asabah (agnatic heirs), mirrors the inheritance priority, and adds critical safeguards—especially for young girls—to protect their welfare. The commentary also introduces Abu Hanifah's distinctive view that non-agnatic relatives (dhawu al-arham) may claim custody by analogy to marriage authority.

Guided Path
1
Identify the triggering scenario
When no female relative from the earlier list (mother, grandmother, etc.) is available or qualified, custody shifts to male relatives. The child is then placed with the most entitled male agnate.
The absence of a female custodian is a precondition.
2
List the 'asabah in inheritance order
Start with the father, then paternal grandfather, then full brother, then half-brothers, then paternal uncles and their sons, and so on. Each closer relative excludes more distant ones.
This order is derived by qiyas from inheritance rules.
3
Apply preference among equals
When two or more 'asabah are equally close (e.g., two full cousins), the more pious (al-awra') is chosen, then the older (al-asan).
Piety (taqwa) and age are secondary criteria.
4
Recognize restrictions for female children
A young girl cannot be placed with a male relative who is not her mahram (e.g., paternal cousin). Additionally, the custodian must be trustworthy ('amin) and not openly sinful (fasiq), to avoid fitnah.
For boys, these restrictions are relaxed.
5
Consider the dhawu al-arham position
If no 'asabah exists, Abu Hanifah permits custody to uterine relatives (dhawu al-arham), analogizing to their authority in marriage (wilayah al-inkah). This is a distinctive Hanafi ruling.
Other schools generally deny this.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم العصبات بترتيبهم : [ أي بعد هؤلاء المذكورات الأحق بالولد العصبات (ع)] أي إن لم يكن للصبي امرأة من أهل الحضانة، واختصم فيه الرجال، فأولاهم بالحضانة العصبات بترتيب الإرث يقدم الأقرب فالأقرب، فيقدم الأب ثم الجد، ثم الأخ الشقيق، ثم الأب ثم بنوه كذلك، ثم العم، ثم بنوه، وإذا اجتمعوا فالأورع ثم الأسن، غير أن الصغيرة لا تدفع إلى غير المحرم من الأقارب كابن العم ولا للأم التي ليس بمأمونة، ولا للعصبة الفاسق تحرزاً عن الفتنة، بخلاف الغلام، وإذا لم يكن للصغير عصبة يدفع إلى ذوي الأرحام عند أبي حنيفة؛ لأن لهم ولاية الإنكاح عنده، فكذا الحضانة (عيني، مستخلص، فتح)
Then the 'asabah in their order: [i.e., after the aforementioned women, the 'asabah are more entitled to the child]. Meaning: if the boy has no woman from among the people of custody, and the men dispute, then the most entitled to custody are the 'asabah in the order of inheritance: the nearest is given priority over the nearer. So the father is given priority, then the grandfather, then the full brother, then the father [i.e., paternal uncles] then his sons likewise, then the paternal uncle, then his sons. And when they gather, the most pious then the eldest. However, the young girl is not handed over to a relative who is not a mahram, like the son of the paternal uncle, nor to a mother who is not trustworthy, nor to a fasiq 'asabah, to avoid fitnah – contrary to the boy. And if the child has no 'asabah, he is handed over to dhawu al-arham according to Abu Hanifah because, in his view, they have the authority of marriage (wilayah al-inkah), so likewise custody.
Source pages p. 521

Commentary
This passage is an expansion (sharh) of the matn statement: 'then the 'asabah in their order.' The commentary clarifies that when no female relative from the earlier list is available, male relatives from the 'asabah (agnatic heirs) are entitled to custody, following the same order of priority as in inheritance: the closest in kinship first. Thus, the father is first, then the grandfather, then the full brother, then the paternal uncles and their sons, etc. Among those of equal degree, the more pious is preferred, then the older. However, there are restrictions: a female child cannot be given to a non-mahram male relative like the cousin (ibn 'amm), nor to an untrustworthy mother, nor to a fasiq 'asabah, to avoid temptation. For a boy, these restrictions are less stringent. An important point: Abu Hanifah allows custody to dhawu al-arham (uterine relatives) if no 'asabah exist, analogizing to their authority in marriage (wilayah al-inkah). This is a distinctive Hanafi position.

Key Terms
عصبات (ʿaṣabāt): Agnatic male relatives who inherit as residuaries; they are also entitled to custody.
ذوو الأرحام (dhawu al-arḥām): Uterine relatives (female-connected or without agnatic tie) who are not 'asabah; they have a secondary claim to custody according to Abu Hanifah.
محرم (maḥram): A male relative whom a woman cannot marry due to blood or marriage ties; custody of a young girl is restricted to mahram male relatives.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The full brother is more entitled than the grandfather.
B) The grandfather is more entitled than the full brother.
C) They are equally entitled.
D) The father must be present first, then the brother.

Key Takeaway
Custody among male agnatic relatives follows the order of inheritance: the closest in kinship is most entitled, with additional conditions of piety and mahram for girls, and Abu Hanifah extends custody to uterine relatives when no agnates exist.

Duration of Custody and the Ruling for Slave Women
Sets age limits for custody of boys/girls and excludes slave women.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi criteria for when custody of a boy and a girl transfers to the father, and explain why a slave mother has no custody right until freed.

Core Idea
Custody ends at functional independence (boys ~7) or puberty/desire (girls ~9); slave mothers have no right until emancipation.

The classical Hanafi texts carefully define when a mother's right to custody ends. For a boy, it is when he can care for himself; for a girl, it is when she reaches puberty or the age of desire. These rules are not arbitrary but based on the child's needs and the parent's roles. Additionally, the text clarifies that slave women, including umm walad, have no custody rights until they are freed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main rulings
The passage gives three distinct rulings: (a) custody duration for a boy, (b) custody duration for a girl, and (c) the exclusion of slave women (including umm walad). Each has its own condition and proof.
Keep these separate in analysis.
2
Analyze the condition for a boy
The boy's custody ends when he is 'yastaghni' (independent). The text defines independence as eating, drinking, and cleaning alone, and estimates it at seven years. The proof is the hadith commanding prayer at seven, implying ability to perform purification.
The age is a presumptive benchmark, not absolute.
3
Analyze the condition for a girl
The mother retains custody until the girl reaches puberty (menstruation). However, Imam Muhammad holds that at the age of desire (≈9 years) the father becomes more entitled. This is the fatwa today due to widespread corruption.
Intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf exists between Imam Muhammad and the two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf).
4
Compare with other schools
The Maliki school holds that the girl stays with the mother until marriage and consummation, a significantly longer period. This highlights the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence) of the differing views.
The Hanafi position is more father-centered after puberty or desire.
5
Apply the slave women ruling
Slave women and umm walad have no custody rights because they lack personal autonomy (wilayah) and are occupied with the master's service. Only after full emancipation do they gain custody. The mukatabah (slave under contract) is a special case with right over her child born during kitabah.
The ruling is based on inability and lack of legal capacity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حتى يستغني، وقدر بسبع سنين، وبها حتى تحيض وغيرهما أحق بها حتى تشتهي، أي للاستغناء عن النساء ولا حق للأمة وأم الولد ما لم تعتقا. يستغني : أي يأكل وحده ويشرب وحده ويستنجي وحده. (ع) وقدر بسبع سنين : [ أي إذا بلغ الغلام هذا السن استغنى عنهن وعليه الفتوى (ط)] بيان لمدة الحضانة وفسره القدوري بأن يأكل ويشرب ويستنجي وحده، وقدره الخصاف بسبع سنين اعتباراً للغالب، وهو قريب من الأول بل عينه؛ لأن الولد إذا بلغ سبع سنين يستنجي وحده، ألا ترى إلى ما روي عنه عليه أنه قال: "مروا صبيانكم بالصلاة إذا بلغوا سبعاً"، والأمر بالصلاة لا يكون إلا بعد القدرة على الطهارة، وقيل: بتسع سنين؛ لأنه لا يستغني قبل ذلك عادة والفتوى على قول الخصاف رمز الحقائق: ۳۰۹/۱] لأنه إذا استغنى يحتاج إلى التأديب بآداب الرجال، والأب يقدر على التأديب والتعليم، وإن اختلفا في سنه فقال الأب: ابن سبع وقالت الأم: ابن ست، فإن استغنى بما ذكرنا دفع إلى الأب وإلا فلا. (مستخلص، عيني) تشتهي: بأن تبلغ تسعا وبه يفتى وعن محمد أن الحكم في الأم والجدة كذلك. [رمز الحقائق: ۳۰۹/۱] (فتح) ما لم تعتقا: لعجزهما عن ذلك باشتغالهما بخدمة المولى. (ع)
Until he becomes independent, and it is estimated at seven years. And with her until she menstruates, and others are more entitled to her until she desires, i.e., for the independence from women. And no right for the slave woman and umm al-walad as long as they are not freed. 'Yastaghni' (becomes independent) means: he eats alone, drinks alone, and cleans himself alone. And it is estimated at seven years: [i.e., when the boy reaches this age, he becomes independent of them, and upon it is the fatwa]. It is an explanation of the duration of custody, and al-Quduri interpreted it as eating, drinking, and cleaning himself alone. Al-Khassaf estimated it at seven years considering the usual case, and it is close to the first but rather specifies it, because when the boy reaches seven he cleans himself alone – do you not see what was narrated from him that he said: 'Command your children to pray when they reach seven'? And the command to pray is only after ability to perform purification. And it is said: at nine years, because he does not become independent before that usually. And the fatwa is on the saying of al-Khassaf, because when he becomes independent he needs discipline with the manners of men, and the father is capable of discipline and teaching. And if they differ about his age, the father saying 'my son is seven' and the mother saying 'six', then if he is independent as described, he is handed to the father, otherwise not. 'And with her until she menstruates' means: the mother and grandmother are more entitled to the custody of the young girl until she reaches puberty, because after independence she needs to learn the arts of spinning, cooking, washing, etc., and the mother is more capable of that. When she reaches puberty she needs marriage and maintenance, and the father is more capable of that and he has the authority of marriage. And from Muhammad: if she reaches the age of desire, then the father is more entitled, and with it fatwa is given in our time due to the abundance of corrupt people. And if she reaches eleven years, she has reached the age of desire in their saying. Abu al-Layth estimated it at nine years. And according to Malik: she stays with the mother until she marries and her husband enters to her. 'And others are more entitled to her' means: the women other than the mother and grandmother are more entitled to the girl until she desires, i.e., until she reaches an age where she could be sexually active, which varies by situation of plumpness, leanness, strength, weakness, ugliness, beauty, because others cannot employ her legally. Its meaning is that the father's teaching only happens through employment, and employing a young girl is not permissible for other than the mother and grandmother. In al-Jami' al-Saghir: until she becomes independent. In al-Kafi: if a man divorces his wife and has from her a daughter of eleven years, and the wife keeps her and goes out of her house every time, leaving the daughter neglected, then he may take her. 'Desire' means reaching nine years, and with it fatwa is given. And from Muhammad: the ruling for the mother and grandmother is likewise. 'And no right for the slave woman' means: if the master marries off his slave woman or umm walad and they give birth, they have no right in custody due to their inability along with service to the master, and because in custody there is a kind of guardianship, and they have no guardianship over themselves, so others are more entitled. And their master is more entitled to the child if the child is in slavery. And if the child is free, then custody goes to his free relatives. And when they are freed, they have the right of custody over their children because they and their children are free. And the right is established for the mukatabah (slave under contract) over her child born during kitabah, unlike the child born before it. 'As long as they are not freed' due to their inability because they are occupied with the master's service.
Source pages p. 522

Commentary
This passage is an extensive commentary on the matn's statements about the duration of custody for boys and girls and the exclusion of slave women. For a boy, custody ends when he becomes independent – able to eat, drink, and clean himself alone, estimated at seven years according to al-Khassaf (the relied-upon view). A hadith about commanding children to pray at seven supports this age because prayer requires ability to perform wudu. If there is disagreement between parents about the boy's age, the actual independence is assessed. For a girl, the mother and grandmother retain custody until she reaches puberty (menstruation), because she needs to learn domestic skills from the mother. After puberty, the father is more entitled because he can arrange her marriage and provide protection. However, according to Imam Muhammad, if she reaches the age of desire (estimated at nine or eleven), the father is more entitled; this is the fatwa in later times due to widespread corruption. For female relatives other than the mother and grandmother (e.g., aunts), they are entitled until the girl reaches an age where she could be sexually active ('tastahi'), which varies by individual. The passage also clarifies that teaching a girl by the father is only through employment (using her in tasks), but employing a girl who is not a mahram is not allowed for non-mahram male. Finally, slave women and umm walad have no right to custody until they are freed, because they lack authority over themselves and are occupied with the master's service. Once freed, they gain custody. A mukatabah (slave under contract of manumission) has a superior right over her child born during the kitabah period. This ruling is based on the principle that custody requires a form of guardianship (wilayah), which a slave does not have over herself.

Key Terms
يستغني (yastaghni): The boy's independence in basic self-care (eating, drinking, cleaning), marking the end of maternal custody.
تشتهي (tastahi): The girl reaching an age where she could be the subject of sexual desire, typically 9 years, ending custody of non-mahram female relatives.
أمة (amah): A female slave; she has no custody rights over her child until freed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) 5 years
B) 7 years
C) 9 years
D) 10 years

Key Takeaway
Custody duration depends on the child's need: independence for boys, puberty/desire for girls; slave mothers have no custody until freed.

Custody of Male Child Until Self-Sufficiency at Seven Years
Ruling on duration of male child custody with dalil

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the duration of a male child's custody, explain the evidence from the hadith, and apply the criterion of independence in a dispute.

Core Idea
A male child stays with his mother until he can eat, drink, and clean himself alone – estimated at seven years.

This lesson addresses a key question in family law: how long does a mother's right to custody (hadhanah) of her son last? The passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq, with its commentary, gives the Hanafi ruling: custody ends when the child becomes independent in basic self-care. The age of seven is a standard estimate, proven by a hadith about the command to pray. Understanding this ruling requires weighing textual evidence against practical realities and resolving a scholarly ikhtilaf.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and the act
The passage rules on the duration of a male child's custody by his mother. The act is the mother's right of hadhanah. The ruling is that this right ends when the child 'becomes independent' (yastaghni).
Independence is defined as eating, drinking, and performing istinja' alone.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof cited is the hadith: 'Command your children to pray when they reach seven years.' This is a Sunnah ahad (single-chain transmission), so its thubut is zanni.
The hadith is from Sunan Abi Dawud.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The command to pray implies the ability to perform purification (tahara), which includes istinja'. Therefore, the child at seven is capable of cleaning himself, which is part of independence. Thus, the age of seven indicates self-sufficiency.
This is an inference based on necessity (lazim).
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Al-Khassaf estimated seven years; some said nine years because a child usually is not independent before that. The fatwa is on al-Khassaf's view because the hadith provides a clear marker, and after independence the child needs male discipline which the father can provide.
The tarjih authority here is al-Marghinani in al-Hidayah.
5
Apply the ruling in a dispute
If parents disagree on the child's age (father says seven, mother says six), the decisive factor is actual independence, not the number. If the child is independent, he goes to the father; otherwise, he stays with the mother.
This shows the criterion is real capability, not a rigid age.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الحضانة حتى يستغني، وقدر بسبع سنين، يستغني : أي يأكل وحده ويشرب وحده ويستنجي وحده. (ع) وقدر بسبع سنين : [ أي إذا بلغ الغلام هذا السن استغنى عنهن وعليه الفتوى (ط)] بيان لمدة الحضانة وفسره القدوري بأن يأكل ويشرب ويستنجي وحده، وقدره الخصاف بسبع سنين اعتباراً للغالب، وهو قريب من الأول بل عينه؛ لأن الولد إذا بلغ سبع سنين يستنجي وحده، ألا ترى إلى ما روي عنه عليه أنه قال: "مروا صبيانكم بالصلاة إذا بلغوا سبعاً، والأمر بالصلاة لا يكون إلا بعد القدرة على الطهارة، وقيل: بتسع سنين؛ لأنه لا يستغني قبل ذلك عادة والفتوى على قول الخصاف [رمز الحقائق: ۳۰۹/۱] لأنه إذا استغنى يحتاج إلى التأديب بآداب الرجال، والأب يقدر على التأديب والتعليم، وإن اختلفا في سنه فقال الأب: ابن سبع وقالت الأم: ابن ست، فإن استغنى بما ذكرنا دفع إلى الأب وإلا فلا. (مستخلص، عيني)
Chapter of Custody: Until he becomes independent, and it is estimated at seven years. 'He becomes independent' means he eats alone, drinks alone, and cleans himself alone. (ع) 'And it is estimated at seven years' means if the boy reaches this age he becomes independent of them, and the fatwa is upon it. It is an explanation of the period of custody. Al-Quduri interpreted it as eating, drinking, and cleaning alone. Al-Khassaf estimated it at seven years considering the norm, and it is close to the first opinion, indeed it specifies it; because when a boy reaches seven years he cleans himself alone. Do you not see what was narrated from him (peace be upon him) that he said: 'Command your children to pray when they reach seven years,' and the command to pray is only after ability to purify. And it was said: nine years, because he does not become independent before that usually. The fatwa is on the saying of al-Khassaf, because when he becomes independent he needs discipline with the manners of men, and the father is capable of discipline and education. If they differ about his age: the father says he is seven, the mother says he is six, then if he is independent as we mentioned he is given to the father, otherwise not.
Source pages p. 522

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the duration of a male child's custody (hadhanah). The matn states that custody lasts until the child becomes independent (yastaghni). The sharh explains independence as being able to eat, drink, and perform istinja' (cleansing after relieving oneself) alone. The age is estimated at seven years by al-Khassaf, relying on a hadith that commands prayer at seven, implying ability to perform wudu and thus istinja'. There is an ikhtilaf: some say nine years. The fatwa is on seven years. The rationale: after independence, the child needs male discipline and education, for which the father is better suited. If parents disagree on the child's age, the criterion is actual independence, not a fixed number.

Key Terms
حَضَانَة (hadānah): Custody of a child, the right to raise and care for the child.
يَسْتَغْنِي (yastaghnī): The child becomes independent in basic self-care: eating, drinking, and istinja' alone.
قُدِّرَ بِسَبْعِ سِنِينَ (quddira bi-sabʿi sinīn): Estimated at seven years as the default age of independence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Reaching the age of seven years
B) Becoming able to eat, drink, and perform istinja' alone
C) Reaching puberty
D) The father demanding custody

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi ruling on custody of a male child ends when he is self-sufficient in basic care, estimated at seven years based on the hadith of prayer, with actual ability being the decisive factor.

Duration of Custody for Female Child: Mother vs Other Women
Ruling on duration of female child custody with differences

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two distinct custody durations for a female child under Hanafi law, explain the intra-Hanafi difference between the mother/grandmother and other women, and apply the mufta bihi ruling in a contemporary scenario.

Core Idea
Custody of a girl: mother/grandmother until menstruation; other women until sexual desire (usually age 9).

The custody of a female child is not indefinite; the Hanafi school distinguishes between the mother (and grandmother) and other female relatives. This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq explains the different endpoints: menstruation for the mother, and the onset of sexual desire for others, with a significant intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf that affects modern practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of custodians
Distinguish between the mother/grandmother (الأم والجدة) and other female relatives (غيرهما). The text rules separately for each.
The mother/grandmother have a stronger right that lasts until puberty.
2
Apply the criteria for each category
For mother/grandmother: custody ends at tubligh (menstruation). For others: custody ends when the girl becomes 'tashtahi' (sexually desired), which is a flexible concept.
The text explains the underlying reasoning: after puberty, the girl needs marriage skills, which the father is better equipped to provide.
3
Analyze the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Imam Muhammad holds that once the girl reaches the limit of desire (shahwah), the father becomes entitled, even as against the mother/grandmother. This view is adopted for fatwa in our time due to widespread corruption.
Abu al-Layth estimated the age of desire at nine years, which is the operative rule today.
4
Determine the mufta bihi and practical application
The contemporary fatwa follows Imam Muhammad's view: custody ends at age nine for all female relatives. The father may take custody earlier if the girl is neglected or in corrupt surroundings.
The text also mentions that employing the girl (istikhdam) is only permissible for mother/grandmother, reinforcing the distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبها حتى تحيض وغيرهما أحق بها حتى تشتهي، أي للاستغناء عن النساء. وبها حتى تحيض: أي الأم والجدة أحق بحضانة الصغيرة حتى تبلغ؛ لأن بعد الاستغناء تحتاج إلى معرفة آداب من الغزل والطبخ والغسل ونحوها، والأم أقدر على ذلك، فإذا بلغت تحتاج إلى التزويج والصيانة، والأب أقدر عليها، وإليه ولاية التزويج وعن محمد: إذا بلغت حد الشهوة فالأب أحق به، وبه يفتى في زماننا لكثرة الفساق، وإذا بلغت إحدى عشرة سنة، فقد بلغت حد الشهوة في قولهم وقدره أبو الليث بتسع سنين، وعليه مالك: تمكث عند الأم حتى تتزوج ويدخل بها الزوج [رمز الحقائق: ٣٠٩/١] (مص) وغيرهما أحق بها: أي النسوة اللاتي سوى الأم والجدة أحق بالجارية حتى تشتهي بأن تبلغ مبلغاً يجامع مثلها فإنه يختلف باختلاف الأحوال من السمن والهزال والقوة والضعف والقبح والجمال؛ لأن سواهما لا تقدر على استخدامها شرعاً، معناه أن تعليم الأب إنما يحصل بالاستخدام، واستخدام الصغيرة لا يحل لغير الأم والجدة، وفي 'الجامع الصغير': حتى تستغني، وفي 'الكافي' : إذا خلع الرجل امرأته وله منها بنت إحدى عشرة سنة، فضمتها إليها، وتخرج من بيتها كل وقت، فتترك البنت ضائعة، فله أن يأخذها. (مستخلص، عيني) تشتهي: بأن تبلغ تسعا وبه يفتى وعن محمد أن الحكم في الأم والجدة كذلك. (ط)
And with her until she menstruates, and others are more entitled to her until she desires, meaning for independence from women. 'And with her until she menstruates' means the mother and grandmother are more entitled to the custody of the young girl until she reaches puberty; because after independence she needs to learn the manners of spinning, cooking, washing and the like, and the mother is more capable of that. When she reaches puberty, she needs marriage and maintenance, and the father is more capable of that, and to him belongs the authority of marriage. And from Muhammad: if she reaches the limit of desire, then the father is more entitled to her, and by this fatwa is given in our time due to the abundance of corrupt people. If she reaches eleven years, she has reached the limit of desire in their saying, and Abu al-Layth estimated it at nine years. And according to Malik: she stays with the mother until she marries and the husband consummates. 'And others are more entitled to her' means the women other than the mother and grandmother are more entitled to the girl until she desires, i.e., until she reaches an age where someone like her is sexually desired, which varies with circumstances of fatness, thinness, strength, weakness, ugliness, and beauty; because others cannot employ her legally, meaning that the father's education is only achieved through employing her, and employing a young girl is not permissible for anyone other than the mother and grandmother. In al-Jami' al-Saghir: until she becomes independent. In al-Kafi: if a man divorces his wife and he has from her an eleven-year-old daughter, and she takes her and goes out from her house every time, leaving the daughter neglected, then he may take her. 'Desires' means she reaches nine years, and by this fatwa is given, and from Muhammad that the ruling for the mother and grandmother is the same.
Source pages p. 522

Commentary
This passage covers the custody of a female child. The matn distinguishes between two scenarios: (1) the mother and grandmother have custody until the girl reaches puberty (tubligh, i.e., menstruation). After that, the father is more entitled because he can arrange marriage and provide maintenance. (2) Other female relatives (not mother or grandmother) have custody only until the girl reaches sexual maturity (tashtahi), i.e., becomes sexually desirable, which varies by individual. The commentary explains that after puberty, the girl needs instruction in domestic skills, which the mother can best provide. But at puberty, the father becomes responsible for marriage. Imam Muhammad said that if she reaches the limit of desire (shahwah), the father is more entitled, and this is the fatwa today due to widespread corruption. Once she reaches such an age, the father should take custody to protect her. The text also notes that employing a young girl is only permissible for mother and grandmother; others cannot. If a mother takes the girl out excessively and neglects her, the father may take her. The age of desire is generally nine years, and by this fatwa is given.

Key Terms
تَحِيض (tahīḍu): The girl menstruates, indicating puberty (bulugh).
تَشْتَهِي (tashtahī): The girl reaches an age where she is sexually desired (shahwah), generally nine years.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, custody of a female child ends for the mother/grandmother at menstruation, and for other women at the onset of sexual desire (tashtahi), which is operationally set at nine years according to the contemporary fatwa; Imam Muhammad's view makes the father entitled at desire even against the mother in our time.

No Custody for Enslaved Mothers
Ruling denying custody to enslaved women

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that an enslaved mother (amah or umm walad) has no right to custody of her child as long as she remains enslaved, and identify the condition of freedom that triggers the right.

Core Idea
An enslaved mother has no custody until she is freed.

This lesson addresses a specific family law ruling from the Hanafi school: the denial of custody (hadhanah) to mothers who are enslaved, whether they are ordinary slaves (amah) or umm walad (mother of the master's child). The ruling is clear and unconditional: custody rights are suspended until the mother gains her freedom. The commentary explains the rationale based on the mother's incapacity due to service obligations and her lack of guardianship over herself.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties
Recognize the two categories of enslaved mothers: amah (a slave woman not the mother of her master's child) and umm walad (a slave woman who has given birth to her master's child).
Even though the umm walad has certain privileges (e.g., she cannot be sold), she is still enslaved for custody purposes.
2
State the ruling
Memorize the core rule: 'There is no right for the amah and the umm walad as long as they are not freed.' This is the central point of the passage.
The ruling is absolute: no exceptions while enslaved.
3
Note the condition for reversal
Understand that the right to custody is activated upon manumission. Once the mother is freed, she gains the same custody rights as any free mother.
Freedom is a condition (shart) for custody.
4
Consider child's status
If the child is also enslaved, the master has priority over the child. If the child is free (as with an umm walad), custody goes to free maternal relatives.
The child's freedom affects who gets custody in the mother's absence of right.
5
Note the mukatabah exception
A mukatabah (slave with a manumission contract) has priority for custody of a child born during the contract, unlike a child born before.
This is a nuance: the contract gives her a claim even though she is not fully free.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا حق للأمة وأم الولد ما لم تعتقا. ولا حق للأمة: يعني إذا زوج المولى أمته أو أم ولده ثم ولدتا لا حق لهما في الحضانة لعجزهما عنها مع خدمة المولى؛ ولأنه في الحضانة نوع ولاية، ولا ولاية لهما على أنفسهما فعلي غيرهما أولى، ومولاهما أولى بالولد إن كان الصغير في الرق، ولا يفرق إن كانا في ملكه، وإن كان الصغير حراً فالحضانة لأقربائه الأحرار، وإذا اعتقتا كان لهما حق الحضانة في أولادهما؛ لأنهما وأولادهما أحراراً، وإن ثبوت الحق والمكاتبة أحق بولدها المولود في الكتابة بخلاف المولود قبلها. [رمز الحقائق: ۳۰۹/۱] (فتح) ما لم تعتقا: لعجزهما عن ذلك باشتغالهما بخدمة المولى. (ع) الفتوى، وعن بينه وبين أمه
And there is no right for the enslaved woman (amah) and the umm walad as long as they are not freed. 'No right for the enslaved woman' means: if the master marries off his amah or his umm walad and then they give birth, they have no right to custody because they are incapable of it due to serving the master; and because in custody there is a type of guardianship (wilayah), and they have no guardianship over themselves, so over others is even more unlikely. Their master is more entitled to the child if the child is in slavery, and the master separates the child from the mother if they are both in his possession. If the child is free, then custody goes to the child's free relatives. When they become free, they have the right to custody of their children, because both mother and child are free. The right is established for a mukatabah (a slave who has a contract of manumission) to her child born during the contract, unlike a child born before it. 'As long as they are not freed': because they are unable to do so due to being occupied with the master's service.
Source pages p. 522

Commentary
This passage states the ruling that an enslaved mother (either a regular amah or an umm walad) has no right to custody (hadhanah) of her child until she becomes free. The reason is twofold: (1) She is occupied with the service of her master and thus unable to care for the child properly. (2) Custody involves a type of guardianship (wilayah), and an enslaved woman has no guardianship over herself, so she cannot have it over others. If the child is also enslaved, the master has priority over both mother and child. If the child is free (e.g., the mother is umm walad but the child is free upon birth? Actually, a child of an umm walad is free), then the maternal relatives who are free get custody. Once the mother is freed, she regains custody rights. The text also mentions that a mukatabah (a slave contracted to purchase her freedom) has priority over her child born during the contract, unlike a child born before. The garbled final line likely refers to a fatwa or a comparison between the father and mother.

Key Terms
أَمَة (amah): An enslaved woman who is not a mother of her master's child.
أُم وَلَد (umm walad): An enslaved woman who gave birth to her master's child, gaining certain rights.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
An enslaved mother, whether amah or umm walad, has no right to custody until she is freed.

Dhimmi Mother's Custody of Muslim Child
Ruling on dhimmi mother's right to custody of Muslim child

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a dhimmi mother's custody of her Muslim child and explain the conditions under which custody is retained or lost.

Core Idea
A dhimmi mother retains custody of her Muslim child until the child understands religion or is feared to adopt kufr.

This lesson examines a specific scenario in Hanafi family law: the custody rights of a non-Muslim mother (dhimmi) when the father converts to Islam. The Hanafi school, in contrast to other schools, grants her conditional custody based on the child's age and the absence of religious harm. We will analyze the ruling, its proof, and the inter-madhhab dispute.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What exactly is being ruled upon? The act is the custody of a Muslim child by his dhimmi mother. The ruling is that she is more entitled (ahaqq) to custody, but only conditionally.
2
Locate the dalil
What proof does the author cite? The proof is the mother's natural compassion (ashfaq) and the absence of harm to the child's religion.
This is a rational proof (dalil aqli) based on human nature.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
How does the dalil prove the ruling? The mother's compassion indicates that she is more caring, so she is preferred as long as her custody does not lead to religious harm. If harm is feared, custody transfers to the Muslim father.
The balance between compassion and religious protection.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Does any school disagree? The Shafi'i, Maliki, and Hanbali schools (in one narration) hold that a dhimmi mother has no custody right at all due to religious difference.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
Which position is relied upon? The Hanafi position is the mufta bihi because it accommodates the child's welfare without compromising religious integrity.
The ruling applies until the child reaches tamyiz (usually age 7).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والذمية أحق بولدها المسلم ما لم يعقل ديناً. والذميّة أحق بولدها المسلم أي إذا أسلم زوج الذمية وبينهما أولاد صغار، فالذمية أحق بهذه الأولاد ما لم يعقلوا الأديان وما لم يخف أن يألفوا الكفر، وإذا عقل الولد ديناً ينزع منها؛ لاحتمال الضرر. والمرتدة لا حق لها في وعند الشافعي ومالك وأحمد في رواية: لا حق للذمية (مستخلص عيني دينا: لأنها أشفق، فلو عقله أو خيف أن يألف الكفر نزع منها. And the dhimmi (non-Muslim protected woman) is more entitled to her Muslim child as long as he does not understand religion. 'And the dhimmi woman is more entitled to her Muslim child' means: if the husband of the dhimmi woman becomes Muslim and there are young children between them, the dhimmi woman is more entitled to these children as long as they do not understand religions and as long as it is not feared that they will become attached to kufr. When the child understands religion, he is taken from her due to the possibility of harm. And the apostate woman has no right to custody. According to al-Shafi'i, Malik, and Ahmad in one narration, the dhimmi woman has no right at all. (The author says) 'because she is more compassionate; so if he understands or it is feared he will become attached to kufr, he is taken from her.'

Commentary
This passage addresses a scenario where a non-Muslim mother (dhimmi) and a Muslim father have children. If the father converts to Islam while the mother remains dhimmi, the mother retains custody of the children as long as they are too young to understand religious differences (ya'qilu al-adyan). The reason is that she is more compassionate (ashfaq). However, if the child reaches an age where he can understand religion, or if there is fear that he may become attached to kufr, custody is transferred to the father to avoid spiritual harm. The text also notes that an apostate (murtaddah) mother has no custody rights. The author mentions that Shafi'i, Malik, and Ahmad (in one narration) hold that a dhimmi mother has no right to custody at all, but the Hanafi position is as stated, prioritizing her compassion as long as no religious harm occurs.

Key Terms
ذِمِّيَّة (dhimmīyah): A non-Muslim woman living under Islamic protection (dhimma).
يَعْقِلُ دِيناً (ya'qilu dīnan): The child reaches an age of understanding religious differences (tamyiz).

No Choice for Child in Custody (Hanafi View)
Inter-madhhab dispute on whether a child can choose a guardian

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school denies the child's right to choose between parents in custody matters and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
A child's choice is unreliable — Hanafis prioritize the child's welfare over his fleeting whims.

The question of whether a discerning child (mumayyiz) can choose which parent to live with after the custody period is a classic inter-madhhab dispute. In this lesson, you will examine the Hanafi position, its evidence, and the Shafi'i challenge, as well as the Hanafi response. The debate centers on the interpretation of a prophetic hadith and the child's capacity to perceive his own best interest.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi position
State the Hanafi ruling on child custody choice after the period of hadanah. Note that it applies regardless of gender or discernment.
The Hanafi school, along with Malik, holds that the child has no choice.
2
Identify the opposing school and its proof
Identify the Shafi'i position and the primary evidence they cite: the hadith of Abu Hurayrah where the Prophet gave a child a choice.
The Shafi'i school uses this hadith to argue for the child's right to choose at tamyiz.
3
Examine the hadith critically
Read the hadith as quoted in the passage. Note its wording and context as presented.
The Hanafi response will challenge the applicability of this hadith.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
List the Hanafi objections: the child is legally incompetent, the hadith does not specify age, the child may have been an adult, or divinely guided.
Also note the consistency argument regarding choice of faith.
5
Determine the practical consequence
State the thamarah al-khilaf: how the ruling differs in practice between the schools.
Hanafi: father gets custody automatically after mother's period; Shafi'i: child may choose mother.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا خيار للولد [مميزاً أو لا، غلاماً أو جارية عندنا بعد انقضاء الحضانة. (ط،ع)] وبه قال مالك وهو مذهبنا، سواء كان الولد مميزاً أو لا، غلاماً أو جارية، وقال الشافعي: إذا صار مميزاً خير بين الأبوين؛ لما روى أبو هريرة الله أن امرأة جاءت إلى رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم فقالت: إن زوجي يريد أن يذهب بابنه، وقد سقاني من بئر أبي عتبة وقد نفعني فقال زوجها أتحاقني أي تخاصمني في ولدي، فقال عليه: هذا أبوك وهذه أمك فخذ بيد أيهما شئت، فأخذ بيد أمه فانطلقت به. الحد ولنا: أنه صغير غير رشيد، ولا عارف بمصلحته فلا يعتمد اختياره؛ ولأنه لقصور عقله يختار من عنده الراحة والتخلية، فلا يتحقق النظر، ولا حجة له في الحديث؛ لأنه لم يذكر فيه الفرق، فالظاهر أنها كانت في صحبته؛ لقولها: 'إن زوجي'، ويحتمل أنه كان بالغاً بل هو الظاهر؛ لأن الذي يسقي من البئر هو البالغ، وليس فيه دليل على أنه يخير في السبع؛ لأنه ليس في الحديث ذكر عمره، أو لأنه وفق لبركة دعائه عليه، لاختيار الأنظر، فلا يقاس عليه غيره، وقال أحمد: إذا بلغ سبع سنين يخير الغلام وتسلم الجارية إلى الأب من غير تخيير، ومن العجب أنهم لا يعتبرون إيمانه، وهو اختياره لربه، وهو نفع له، ثم يعتبرون اختياره أحد الأبوين وهو ضرر عليه، هذا خلف. (فتح، عيني)
And there is no choice for the child, whether discerning or not, boy or girl, in our view after the period of custody ends. This is the view of Malik and it is our madhhab, whether the child is discerning or not, boy or girl. Al-Shafi'i said: when the child becomes discerning, he is given choice between the parents, because Abu Hurayrah narrated that a woman came to the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) and said: 'My husband wants to take my son, and he has given me water from the well of Abu 'Utbah and benefited me.' Her husband said: 'Do you dispute with me about my son?' He (peace be upon him) said: 'This is your father and this is your mother, take the hand of whichever you wish.' So he took his mother's hand and left with her. Our argument: The child is a minor, not discerning (rashid), and does not know his best interest, so his choice cannot be relied upon. Because of deficiency in intellect, he chooses the one who gives him comfort and play, so no proper consideration is achieved. There is no proof in the hadith, because it does not mention the child's age; apparently he was under the mother's care due to her saying 'my husband.' Also it is likely the child was an adult, because the one who draws water from the well is an adult. There is no evidence that a child of seven is given choice, because the hadith does not mention his age. Or perhaps the child was guided by the blessing of the Prophet's prayer to choose the more beneficial. So others cannot be analogized to him. Ahmad said: if a boy reaches seven, he is given choice; the girl is handed to the father without choice. It is strange that they do not consider his faith (iman), which is his choice of his Lord and is beneficial to him, yet they consider his choice of one parent which is harmful to him. This is inconsistent.
Source pages p. 523

Commentary
This passage presents an inter-madhhab dispute regarding whether a child who has reached the age of discretion (tamyiz) may choose which parent to live with after the period of custody (hadhanah) ends. The Hanafi school, along with Malik, holds that the child has no choice at all, whether the child is discerning or not, and whether boy or girl. The reasoning is that a child is not legally competent to recognize his own best interest (maslahah). He will choose based on immediate pleasure (raha wa takhliyah) rather than long-term benefit. The Shafi'i school, based on a hadith where the Prophet gave a child a choice between his parents, holds that once the child reaches tamyiz, he may choose. The Hanafi response critiques the hadith: (1) The child in the hadith was likely already an adult (baligh) because he was drawing water from a well. (2) Even if the child was young, the narration does not specify his age, so it cannot be used as a general rule. (3) The child's choice may have been guided by divine blessing (barakat du'a) to choose the better option, so it is not a precedent for all children. The Hanafis also ridicule the inconsistency of those who give a child choice over his worldly guardian but not over his faith (iman), which is more important. The commentary also mentions Ahmad's compromise: a boy of seven is given choice, but a girl is not. The Hanafi position is the relied-upon view (mufta bihi) in the school.

Key Terms
خِيَار (khiyār): The right of the child to choose which parent to live with.
مُمَيِّز (mumayyiz): A child who has reached the age of discretion (tamyiz), generally around seven years, able to understand basic interests.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when does a child have the right to choose between parents for custody?
A) When the child reaches seven years
B) When the child reaches puberty
C) When the child becomes discerning (mumayyiz)
D) The child never has a choice

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that a child, even if discerning, has no right to choose between parents for custody; the father automatically gains custody after the mother's custody period ends.

The Divorced Mother's Right to Relocate with Her Child
Ruling on mother's travel with child after divorce with conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a divorced mother's travel with her child and identify the conditions under which she may do so.

Core Idea
A divorced mother may relocate with her child only to her pre-marriage hometown if the marriage took place there, to prevent harm to the father.

After divorce, custody does not give the mother unlimited freedom to move the child. The Hanafi school balances the mother's rights with the father's right to maintain a relationship with his child. This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq explains the specific conditions under which the mother may travel.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its default ruling
The act is a divorced mother's travel with her child to a different location after her 'iddah. The default ruling is prohibition because it causes harm (idrar) to the father, who would lose the ability to visit and train the child.
The child is in her custody (hadhanah), but that does not give her an absolute right to relocate.
2
Extract the exception
The exception is travel to her original hometown (watan) where the husband married her. This is permitted because the husband implicitly accepted this place as a potential residence when he married her.
The commentary quotes 'Ali: 'Whoever marries in a place, he is from its people.'
3
Analyze the conditions for the exception
Two conditions must be met: (1) the destination must be her watan, and (2) the marriage must have taken place there. If either is missing, she cannot move. However, if the distance is negligible (same-day round trip), movement is allowed absolutely within Dar al-Islam.
The 'most correct view' (al-ashahh) also requires both conditions for a move to a village (qarya) from a city (misr).
4
Distinguish the Hanafi position from other schools
Other schools (the 'three' – likely Shafi'i, Maliki, Hanbali) use a fixed distance limit of 16 farsakhs (approx. 48 miles) for permissibility. The Hanafi school uses harm (darar) to the father as the criterion, which is more flexible and context-dependent.
The Hanafi approach aligns with the overarching principle of preventing harm (la darara wa la dirara).
5
Apply to sub-cases
Consider scenarios: moving to a village, or moving by a grandmother. The ruling is stricter for villages (requires both conditions). For relatives other than the mother (e.g., grandmother), relocation requires the father's permission regardless.
This shows the specificity of the mother's privilege based on her unique relationship.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا تسافر مطلقة بولدها إلا إلى وطنها وقد نكحها ثمه. ولا تسافر مطلقة إلخ أي إذا أرادت المطلقة بعد انقضاء العدة أن تخرج بولدها من المصر فليس لها ذلك إن كان أصل النكاح في مصر هي فيه؛ لما فيه من الإضرار بالأب، إلا أن تخرج به إلى وطنها الذي تزوجها الزوج فيه؛ لأنه التزم المقام فيه عرفاً وشرعاً، قال علي: 'من تزوج ببلد فهو منهم'. وإن لم يكن وطناً لها، ولا التزوج واقعا فيه ليس لها أن تنقله، وكذا لو كان وطناً لها، ولم يقع التزوج فيه، وعند الثلاثة: ليس لها السفر إلى ما فوق ستة عشر فرسخاً، ولها ذلك إلى ما دونه، ويستثنى دار الحرب، وإن كان وطناً لها، وقد تزوج فيها؛ لأن في ذلك إضراراً بالولد، وهذا الذي ذكرنا ما إذا كان بين الموضعين تفاوت وإن تقاربا بحيث يتمكن من مطالعة ولده في يوم ويرجع إلى أهله فيه قبل الليل جاز لها النقل إليه مطلقا في دار الإسلام، ولا يشترط فيه وقوع التزوج، ولا كونه وطناً إلا إلى قرية من مصر؛ لكون الولد يتخلق بأخلاق أهل القرى، فلا تملك ذلك إلا أن تكون وطناً لها، ووقع العقد فيها في الأصح، وهذا الحكم في الأم خاصة، وليس لغيرها حتى الجدة أن تنقله إلا بإذن الأب. (مستخلص، عيني) بولدها من بلدة إلى أخرى بينهما تفاوت لما فيه من الإضرار بالولد. (ط،ع) ثمه: أي في وطنها الذي تزوجها فيه، فلو إلى غير وطنها أو إليه وقد نكحها في غيره فلا. (ط،ع)
And a divorced woman (muttallaqah) shall not travel with her child except to her hometown (watan) in which the husband married her. 'And a divorced woman shall not travel...' i.e., when the divorced woman after the expiry of her 'iddah wants to take her child out of the city, she does not have that right if the original marriage took place in that city, because of harm to the father, unless she takes him to her hometown in which the husband married her, because he committed to residence there customarily and legally. 'Ali said: 'Whoever marries in a place, he is from its people.' If it is not her hometown, and the marriage did not take place there, she may not move him. Likewise if it is her hometown but the marriage did not take place there. According to the three (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad? Or the three other schools? Context suggests the three eponyms of other schools?) She may not travel more than sixteen farsakhs; she may travel less. Dar al-harb is excepted. Even if it is her hometown and she married there, because that would harm the child. This ruling applies when there is a distance between the two places. If they are close such that he can see his child in a day and return before night, she may move him absolutely in Dar al-Islam, without requiring that the marriage took place there or that it is her hometown, except if moving to a village (qarya) from a city (misr); because the child would adopt village manners, so she does not have the right unless it is her hometown and the contract took place there according to the most correct view. This ruling is specific to the mother; no one else, not even the grandmother, may move the child without the father's permission. (The summary: 'with her child from one town to another where there is a distance, due to harm to the child.') 'Thamma' means: her hometown in which he married her; if to a place other than her hometown or to it but he married her elsewhere, she may not.
Source pages p. 523

Commentary
This passage deals with the right of a divorced mother (after her 'iddah) to travel with her child from the city where the marriage took place. The general rule is that she cannot move the child to a distant location because it causes harm (idrar) to the father, who would lose the ability to visit and train the child. The exception is if she moves to her original hometown (watan) where the husband married her; since the husband implicitly accepted that she might return there. The commentary quotes 'Ali: 'Whoever marries in a place, he is from its people.' If the mother wants to move to a place that is neither her hometown nor the location of the marriage, she cannot. If the two places are close (facilitating same-day round trip), she may move without restriction. For a village (qarya) vs. city (misr), stricter conditions apply. This ruling applies only to the mother; other relatives like the grandmother need the father's permission. The text also mentions that according to the 'three' (likely the three other imams or the Sahibayn? Actually 'al-thalathah' may refer to the other three madhhabs: Shafi'i, Maliki, Hanbali) the distance limit is 16 farsakhs (approx. 48 miles). The Hanafi rule is based on the principle of preventing harm (darar) to the father, as custody (hadhanah) is not a license to deprive the father of his right to supervise the child's upbringing.

Key Terms
وَطَن (watan): The mother's original hometown where she lived before marriage.
إِضْرَار (iḍrār): Harm to the father, which is the basis for preventing relocation.
فَرْسَخ (farsakh): A unit of distance approx. 3 miles or 5.76 km.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Any city within 16 farsakhs
B) Her hometown where the marriage took place
C) Any city in Dar al-Islam
D) She cannot relocate at all

Key Takeaway
A divorced mother may only relocate with her child to her pre-marriage hometown where the marriage took place, to prevent harming the father's right to nurture his child.
