Custody Rights of a Dhimmi Mother: Hanafi vs. Majority
Ruling on custody of a dhimmi mother and inter-madhhab dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the custody of a dhimmi mother, explain the condition that triggers her removal, and evaluate the opposing schools' argument.

Core Idea
A dhimmi mother keeps custody unless the child understands religion or fears adopting disbelief.

This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling on a non-Muslim (dhimmi) mother's right to custody of her Muslim child after the father's conversion to Islam. The Hanafi school grants her this right conditionally, unlike the Shafi'i, Maliki, and Hanbali schools which deny it outright. Understanding the reasoning behind each position clarifies how the schools balance maternal compassion with religious welfare.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify what the Hanafi school holds regarding the dhimmi mother's entitlement to custody. The text says she is entitled as long as the child does not understand religion and no fear of him adopting disbelief exists.
The default is her right; removal is only when religious harm is feared.
2
Identify the opposing schools and their position
Note that al-Shafi'i, Malik, and Ahmad (in one narration) deny the dhimmi mother any right to custody at all. They argue that a non-Muslim cannot be entrusted with a Muslim child's upbringing.
3
Analyze the Hanafi proof (dalil)
The Hanafi proof is based on the principle that the mother is more compassionate, and the child's benefit lies with her unless there is a clear religious risk. The text does not cite a specific hadith but relies on general consideration (istihsan) and the potential harm to the child's faith.
The Hanafi response addresses the opposing argument by limiting the removal to cases of actual risk.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
The practical difference is that under Hanafi law, a dhimmi mother retains custody until the child reaches an age where he can understand religion or there is a fear of him adopting disbelief. Under the opposing schools, the father would get custody immediately after conversion.
This affects many cases in mixed-faith families.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والذمية أحق بولدها المسلم ما لم يعقل ديناً، ولا خيار للولد، ولا تسافر مطلقة بولدها إلا إلى وطنها وقد نكحها ثمه. والذميّة أحق بولدها المسلم أي إذا أسلم زوج الذمية وبينهما أولاد صغار، فالذمية أحق بهذه الأولاد ما لم يعقلوا الأديان وما لم يخف أن يألفوا الكفر، وإذا عقل الولد ديناً ينزع منها؛ لاحتمال الضرر. والمرتدة لا حق لها في وعند الشافعي ومالك وأحمد في رواية: لا حق للذمية (مستخلص عيني دينا: لأنها أشفق، فلو عقله أو خيف أن يألف الكفر نزع منها. (ط، ع) ولا خيار للولد [مميزاً أو لا، غلاماً أو جارية عندنا بعد انقضاء الحضانة. (ط،ع)] وبه قال مالك وهو مذهبنا، سواء كان الولد مميزاً أو لا، غلاماً أو جارية، وقال الشافعي: إذا صار مميزاً خير بين الأبوين؛ لما روى أبو هريرة الله أن امرأة جاءت إلى رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم فقالت: إن زوجي يريد أن يذهب بابنه، وقد سقاني من بئر أبي عتبة وقد نفعني فقال زوجها أتحاقني أي تخاصمني في ولدي، فقال عليه: هذا أبوك وهذه أمك فخذ بيد أيهما شئت، فأخذ بيد أمه فانطلقت به. ولنا: أنه صغير غير رشيد، ولا عارف بمصلحته فلا يعتمد اختياره؛ ولأنه لقصور عقله يختار من عنده الراحة والتخليلة، فلا يتحقق النظر، ولا حجة له في الحديث؛ لأنه لم يذكر فيه الفرق، فالظاهر أنها كانت في صحبته؛ لقولها: "إن زوجي"، ويحتمل أنه كان بالغاً بل هو الظاهر؛ لأن الذي يسقي من البئر هو البالغ، وليس فيه دليل على أنه يخير في السبع؛ لأنه ليس في الحديث ذكر عمره، أو لأنه وفق لبركة دعائه عليه، لاختيار الأنظر، فلا يقاس عليه غيره، وقال أحمد: إذا بلغ سبع سنين يخير الغلام وتسلم الجارية إلى الأب من غير تخيير، ومن العجب أنهم لا يعتبرون إيمانه، وهو اختياره لربه، وهو نفع له، ثم يعتبرون اختياره أحد الأبوين وهو ضرر عليه، هذا خلف. (فتح، عيني) ولا تسافر مطلقة إلخ أي إذا أرادت المطلقة بعد انقضاء العدة أن تخرج بولدها من المصر فليس لها ذلك إن كان أصل النكاح في مصر هي فيه؛ لما فيه من الإضرار بالأب، إلا أن تخرج به إلى وطنها الذي تزوجها الزوج فيه؛ لأنه التزم المقام فيه عرفاً وشرعاً، قال علي: "من تزوج ببلد فهو منهم". وإن لم يكن وطناً لها، ولا التزوج واقعا فيه ليس لها أن تنقله، وكذا لو كان وطناً لها، ولم يقع التزوج فيه، وعند الثلاثة: ليس لها السفر إلى ما فوق ستة عشر فرسخاً، ولها ذلك إلى ما دونه، ويستثنى دار الحرب، وإن كان وطناً لها، وقد تزوج فيها؛ لأن في ذلك إضراراً بالولد، وهذا الذي ذكرنا ما إذا كان بين الموضعين تفاوت وإن تقاربا بحيث يتمكن من مطالعة ولده في يوم ويرجع إلى أهله فيه قبل الليل جاز لها النقل إليه مطلقا في دار الإسلام، ولا يشترط فيه وقوع التزوج، ولا كونه وطناً إلا إلى قرية من مصر؛ لكون الولد يتخلق بأخلاق أهل القرى، فلا تملك ذلك إلا أن تكون وطناً لها، ووقع العقد فيها في الأصح، وهذا الحكم في الأم خاصة، وليس لغيرها حتى الجدة أن تنقله إلا بإذن الأب. (مستخلص، عيني)
The dhimmi woman is more entitled to her Muslim child as long as he does not understand religion, and there is no choice for the child, and a divorced woman may not travel with her child except to her homeland where he married her. The dhimmi woman is more entitled to her Muslim child, meaning if the husband of the dhimmi converts to Islam and they have young children, the dhimmi is more entitled to these children as long as they do not understand religions and as long as it is not feared that they will become accustomed to disbelief. When the child understands religion, he is taken from her due to fear of harm. The apostate has no right. According to al-Shafi'i, Malik, and Ahmad in one narration, the dhimmi has no right (summary from al-'Ayni: because she is more compassionate, so if he understands or it is feared he will accustom to disbelief, he is taken from her). And there is no choice for the child, discerning or not, boy or girl, in our school after the custody period ends. Malik also said this, and it is our school, whether the child is discerning or not, boy or girl. Al-Shafi'i said: when he becomes discerning, he is given choice between the parents, according to the hadith of Abu Hurayra that a woman came to the Messenger of Allah and said: My husband wants to take my son, and he has given me to drink from the well of Abu 'Utba and benefited me. Her husband said: Do you contend with me over my son? He said: This is your father and this is your mother, take the hand of whichever you wish. So he took his mother's hand and left with her. Our proof: He is a minor, not sensible, does not know his own interest, so his choice is not relied upon; and because due to his weak intellect he chooses one who gives him comfort and freedom, so true consideration is not achieved. And there is no proof in the hadith because it does not mention the distinction, so apparently he was in her care due to her saying 'my husband', and it is possible he was an adult, indeed that is apparent because one who drinks from a well is an adult, and there is no evidence that he is given choice at seven years, because the hadith does not mention his age, or because he was guided by the blessing of his prayer to choose the more beneficial, so others are not analogized to him. Ahmad said: when the boy reaches seven years, he is given choice, and the girl is handed over to the father without choice. It is strange that they do not consider his faith, which is his choice for his Lord and is beneficial to him, then they consider his choice of one parent which is harmful to him. This is inconsistent. (Fath, 'Ayni) And a divorced woman may not travel... i.e., when a divorced woman after completing her 'idda wants to take her child out of the city, she may not do so if the original marriage was in a city where she resides, because it harms the father, unless she takes him to her homeland where the husband married her, because he committed to residence there customarily and legally. 'Ali said: 'Whoever marries in a country is considered one of them.' If it is not her homeland, and the marriage did not take place there, she may not move him. Similarly, if it is her homeland but the marriage did not occur there. According to the three [imams], she may not travel more than sixteen farsakhs, but she may travel less. The abode of war is exempted even if it is her homeland and she married there, because it harms the child. What we mentioned applies when there is a significant distance between the two places; if they are close such that he can visit his child and return home before night, she may move him absolutely in the abode of Islam, without the condition of marriage taking place there or it being her homeland, except to a village from the city, because the child acquires the manners of villagers. So she may not do that unless it is her homeland and the contract took place there, in the most correct view. This ruling applies specifically to the mother; no one else, not even the grandmother, may take him without the father's permission. (Mukhtasar, 'Ayni)
Source pages p. 523

Commentary
This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq, within the chapter on custody (hadanah), presents three interrelated rulings. First, it establishes that a non-Muslim (dhimmi) mother is entitled to custody of her young Muslim child as long as the child does not yet comprehend religious differences and there is no fear that the child will adopt disbelief. Once the child reaches an age where he understands religion, he is taken from her to prevent spiritual harm. The commentator notes that according to al-Shafi'i, Malik, and Ahmad in one narration, the dhimmi mother actually has no right to custody at all. The Hanafi position, however, prioritizes the mother's compassion unless religious harm is evident. Second, the text affirms that the child—whether discerning or not, boy or girl—has no choice in choosing a custodian after the period of custody ends (i.e., after the age where he needs a female custodian). This is in opposition to al-Shafi'i, who gives the discerning child a choice based on a hadith. The Hanafi refutation argues that the child is not yet capable of discerning his own interest, and the hadith used by al-Shafi'i does not clearly indicate that the child was young, as the mention of drinking from a well suggests he was older. Ahmad compromises by giving choice only to boys at seven while handing girls to the father. The Hanafi criticism highlights the inconsistency of giving a child choice in harmful matters while not considering his religious choice. Third, the passage restricts the divorced mother's right to travel with the child after her 'idda: she may only take the child to her homeland where the marriage took place, because the father committed to that location. She may not move the child to another city or even to her own homeland if the marriage occurred elsewhere. The Hanafi school allows travel only up to a short distance (less than sixteen farsakhs) according to the three imams, except to a non-Muslim land. However, if the two places are so close that the father can visit and return within a day, she may take the child freely. This ruling applies specifically to the mother; even the grandmother needs the father's permission to move the child.

Key Terms
الحضانة (al-ḥaḍānah): The right to physically care for and raise a young child, typically until the child no longer needs a female custodian.
ذمية (dhimmīyah): A non-Muslim woman living under the protection of an Islamic state, who has a contract of dhimma.
الخيار (al-khiyār): The option or choice given to a child to choose between parents for custody.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Immediately upon the father's conversion to Islam
B) When the child understands religion or it is feared he will adopt disbelief
C) When the child reaches the age of seven
D) When the mother remarries a non-Muslim

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school grants a dhimmi mother custody unless her influence poses a religious risk to the child.

The Child Has No Choice in Custody: Hanafi vs. Shafi'i
Ruling on child's lack of choice in custody and inter-madhhab dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi reasoning for denying the child a choice in custody and critique the Shafi'i position using the Hanafi arguments from the text.

Core Idea
A discerning child cannot choose his custodian; his choice is unreliable and potentially harmful.

This lesson focuses on the second ruling in the passage: whether a child, after reaching an age where custody would normally pass to the father, has the right to choose between parents. The Hanafi school firmly denies this, while the Shafi'i school grants it based on a hadith. Understanding the Hanafi critique reveals their commitment to the child's actual welfare over apparent autonomy.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position on child choice
The text says 'لا خيار للولد' – the child has no choice, whether discerning or not, boy or girl. The custody arrangement is determined by the rules, not the child's preference.
This applies after the period of female custody ends.
2
Identify the opposing school's position
Al-Shafi'i holds that when the child becomes discerning (mumayyiz), he is given the choice between parents. Malik agrees with Hanafis; Ahmad gives choice to boys only at seven.
3
Analyze the Hanafi proof against choice
The Hanafi argue that the child is not yet sensible (ghayr rashid) and does not know his own interest. He may choose based on comfort rather than long-term benefit. The hadith quoted by Shafi'i is dismissed because it does not specify the child's age; the child in the story was likely an adult (evidenced by drinking from a well), and the child's choice was guided by divine blessing, not generalizable.
The Hanafi critique highlights the inconsistency of giving choice in a worldly matter but not in faith.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Under Hanafi law, the father automatically gets custody once the mother's custody period ends (around age 7-9). Under Shafi'i, the discerning child can choose to stay with the mother, potentially extending her custody.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والذمية أحق بولدها المسلم ما لم يعقل ديناً، ولا خيار للولد، ولا تسافر مطلقة بولدها إلا إلى وطنها وقد نكحها ثمه. والذميّة أحق بولدها المسلم أي إذا أسلم زوج الذمية وبينهما أولاد صغار، فالذمية أحق بهذه الأولاد ما لم يعقلوا الأديان وما لم يخف أن يألفوا الكفر، وإذا عقل الولد ديناً ينزع منها؛ لاحتمال الضرر. والمرتدة لا حق لها في وعند الشافعي ومالك وأحمد في رواية: لا حق للذمية (مستخلص عيني دينا: لأنها أشفق، فلو عقله أو خيف أن يألف الكفر نزع منها. (ط، ع) ولا خيار للولد [مميزاً أو لا، غلاماً أو جارية عندنا بعد انقضاء الحضانة. (ط،ع)] وبه قال مالك وهو مذهبنا، سواء كان الولد مميزاً أو لا، غلاماً أو جارية، وقال الشافعي: إذا صار مميزاً خير بين الأبوين؛ لما روى أبو هريرة الله أن امرأة جاءت إلى رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم فقالت: إن زوجي يريد أن يذهب بابنه، وقد سقاني من بئر أبي عتبة وقد نفعني فقال زوجها أتحاقني أي تخاصمني في ولدي، فقال عليه: هذا أبوك وهذه أمك فخذ بيد أيهما شئت، فأخذ بيد أمه فانطلقت به. ولنا: أنه صغير غير رشيد، ولا عارف بمصلحته فلا يعتمد اختياره؛ ولأنه لقصور عقله يختار من عنده الراحة والتخليلة، فلا يتحقق النظر، ولا حجة له في الحديث؛ لأنه لم يذكر فيه الفرق، فالظاهر أنها كانت في صحبته؛ لقولها: "إن زوجي"، ويحتمل أنه كان بالغاً بل هو الظاهر؛ لأن الذي يسقي من البئر هو البالغ، وليس فيه دليل على أنه يخير في السبع؛ لأنه ليس في الحديث ذكر عمره، أو لأنه وفق لبركة دعائه عليه، لاختيار الأنظر، فلا يقاس عليه غيره، وقال أحمد: إذا بلغ سبع سنين يخير الغلام وتسلم الجارية إلى الأب من غير تخيير، ومن العجب أنهم لا يعتبرون إيمانه، وهو اختياره لربه، وهو نفع له، ثم يعتبرون اختياره أحد الأبوين وهو ضرر عليه، هذا خلف. (فتح، عيني) ولا تسافر مطلقة إلخ أي إذا أرادت المطلقة بعد انقضاء العدة أن تخرج بولدها من المصر فليس لها ذلك إن كان أصل النكاح في مصر هي فيه؛ لما فيه من الإضرار بالأب، إلا أن تخرج به إلى وطنها الذي تزوجها الزوج فيه؛ لأنه التزم المقام فيه عرفاً وشرعاً، قال علي: "من تزوج ببلد فهو منهم". وإن لم يكن وطناً لها، ولا التزوج واقعا فيه ليس لها أن تنقله، وكذا لو كان وطناً لها، ولم يقع التزوج فيه، وعند الثلاثة: ليس لها السفر إلى ما فوق ستة عشر فرسخاً، ولها ذلك إلى ما دونه، ويستثنى دار الحرب، وإن كان وطناً لها، وقد تزوج فيها؛ لأن في ذلك إضراراً بالولد، وهذا الذي ذكرنا ما إذا كان بين الموضعين تفاوت وإن تقاربا بحيث يتمكن من مطالعة ولده في يوم ويرجع إلى أهله فيه قبل الليل جاز لها النقل إليه مطلقا في دار الإسلام، ولا يشترط فيه وقوع التزوج، ولا كونه وطناً إلا إلى قرية من مصر؛ لكون الولد يتخلق بأخلاق أهل القرى، فلا تملك ذلك إلا أن تكون وطناً لها، ووقع العقد فيها في الأصح، وهذا الحكم في الأم خاصة، وليس لغيرها حتى الجدة أن تنقله إلا بإذن الأب. (مستخلص، عيني)
The dhimmi woman is more entitled to her Muslim child as long as he does not understand religion, and there is no choice for the child, and a divorced woman may not travel with her child except to her homeland where he married her. The dhimmi woman is more entitled to her Muslim child, meaning if the husband of the dhimmi converts to Islam and they have young children, the dhimmi is more entitled to these children as long as they do not understand religions and as long as it is not feared that they will become accustomed to disbelief. When the child understands religion, he is taken from her due to fear of harm. The apostate has no right. According to al-Shafi'i, Malik, and Ahmad in one narration, the dhimmi has no right (summary from al-'Ayni: because she is more compassionate, so if he understands or it is feared he will accustom to disbelief, he is taken from her). And there is no choice for the child, discerning or not, boy or girl, in our school after the custody period ends. Malik also said this, and it is our school, whether the child is discerning or not, boy or girl. Al-Shafi'i said: when he becomes discerning, he is given choice between the parents, according to the hadith of Abu Hurayra that a woman came to the Messenger of Allah and said: My husband wants to take my son, and he has given me to drink from the well of Abu 'Utba and benefited me. Her husband said: Do you contend with me over my son? He said: This is your father and this is your mother, take the hand of whichever you wish. So he took his mother's hand and left with her. Our proof: He is a minor, not sensible, does not know his own interest, so his choice is not relied upon; and because due to his weak intellect he chooses one who gives him comfort and freedom, so true consideration is not achieved. And there is no proof in the hadith because it does not mention the distinction, so apparently he was in her care due to her saying 'my husband', and it is possible he was an adult, indeed that is apparent because one who drinks from a well is an adult, and there is no evidence that he is given choice at seven years, because the hadith does not mention his age, or because he was guided by the blessing of his prayer to choose the more beneficial, so others are not analogized to him. Ahmad said: when the boy reaches seven years, he is given choice, and the girl is handed over to the father without choice. It is strange that they do not consider his faith, which is his choice for his Lord and is beneficial to him, then they consider his choice of one parent which is harmful to him. This is inconsistent. (Fath, 'Ayni) And a divorced woman may not travel... i.e., when a divorced woman after completing her 'idda wants to take her child out of the city, she may not do so if the original marriage was in a city where she resides, because it harms the father, unless she takes him to her homeland where the husband married her, because he committed to residence there customarily and legally. 'Ali said: 'Whoever marries in a country is considered one of them.' If it is not her homeland, and the marriage did not take place there, she may not move him. Similarly, if it is her homeland but the marriage did not occur there. According to the three [imams], she may not travel more than sixteen farsakhs, but she may travel less. The abode of war is exempted even if it is her homeland and she married there, because it harms the child. What we mentioned applies when there is a significant distance between the two places; if they are close such that he can visit his child and return home before night, she may move him absolutely in the abode of Islam, without the condition of marriage taking place there or it being her homeland, except to a village from the city, because the child acquires the manners of villagers. So she may not do that unless it is her homeland and the contract took place there, in the most correct view. This ruling applies specifically to the mother; no one else, not even the grandmother, may take him without the father's permission. (Mukhtasar, 'Ayni)

Commentary
This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq, within the chapter on custody (hadanah), presents three interrelated rulings. First, it establishes that a non-Muslim (dhimmi) mother is entitled to custody of her young Muslim child as long as the child does not yet comprehend religious differences and there is no fear that the child will adopt disbelief. Once the child reaches an age where he understands religion, he is taken from her to prevent spiritual harm. The commentator notes that according to al-Shafi'i, Malik, and Ahmad in one narration, the dhimmi mother actually has no right to custody at all. The Hanafi position, however, prioritizes the mother's compassion unless religious harm is evident. Second, the text affirms that the child—whether discerning or not, boy or girl—has no choice in choosing a custodian after the period of custody ends (i.e., after the age where he needs a female custodian). This is in opposition to al-Shafi'i, who gives the discerning child a choice based on a hadith. The Hanafi refutation argues that the child is not yet capable of discerning his own interest, and the hadith used by al-Shafi'i does not clearly indicate that the child was young, as the mention of drinking from a well suggests he was older. Ahmad compromises by giving choice only to boys at seven while handing girls to the father. The Hanafi criticism highlights the inconsistency of giving a child choice in harmful matters while not considering his religious choice. Third, the passage restricts the divorced mother's right to travel with the child after her 'idda: she may only take the child to her homeland where the marriage took place, because the father committed to that location. She may not move the child to another city or even to her own homeland if the marriage occurred elsewhere. The Hanafi school allows travel only up to a short distance (less than sixteen farsakhs) according to the three imams, except to a non-Muslim land. However, if the two places are so close that the father can visit and return within a day, she may take the child freely. This ruling applies specifically to the mother; even the grandmother needs the father's permission to move the child.

Key Terms
الحضانة (al-ḥaḍānah): The right to physically care for and raise a young child, typically until the child no longer needs a female custodian.
الخيار (al-khiyār): The option or choice given to a child to choose between parents for custody.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Because the school follows a different hadith

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school denies the child a choice in custody, arguing that the child cannot make a sound decision about his own welfare.

Travel Restrictions on the Custodial Mother After Divorce
Rulings on a divorced mother's travel with her child and comparative fiqh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a divorced mother may travel with her child after 'idda according to the Hanafi school, and explain the rationale behind the restrictions.

Core Idea
A divorced mother cannot travel with her child to a distant place without the father's consent, unless it is to her original homeland where the marriage occurred.

The third ruling in the passage addresses the mother's right to relocate with her child after divorce. The Hanafi school imposes significant restrictions to protect the father's visitation rights and the child's connection to the father. This lesson unpacks the conditions and exceptions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
The text says 'ولا تسافر مطلقة بولدها إلا إلى وطنها وقد نكحها ثمه' – a divorced woman may not travel with her child except to her homeland where her husband married her. This is the default: she cannot take the child away from the father's vicinity.
The harm to the father is the key reason.
2
Define 'homeland' (watan) and the condition of marriage
The permissible travel is only to the place that is her original home (watan) and where the marriage took place. If she wants to go elsewhere, she cannot. The Hanafi school argues the father committed to that location when he married her.
The statement of 'Ali: 'Whoever marries in a country is considered one of them.'
3
Note the exceptions and differences with other schools
The three imams (other schools) allow travel up to 16 farsakhs without restriction. Also, if the distance is short (one day round trip), she may take the child absolutely. The abode of war is always excluded.
4
Identify the scope of the ruling
This restriction applies only to the mother. The grandmother or others cannot take the child without the father's permission.
Even the grandmother may not move the child without consent.

Commentary
This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq, within the chapter on custody (hadanah), presents three interrelated rulings. First, it establishes that a non-Muslim (dhimmi) mother is entitled to custody of her young Muslim child as long as the child does not yet comprehend religious differences and there is no fear that the child will adopt disbelief. The Hanafi position, however, prioritizes the mother's compassion unless religious harm is evident. Ahmad compromises by giving choice only to boys at seven while handing girls to the father. The Hanafi criticism highlights the inconsistency of giving a child choice in harmful matters while not considering his religious choice. Third, the passage restricts the divorced mother's right to travel with the child after her 'idda: she may only take the child to her homeland where the marriage took place, because the father committed to that location. She may not move the child to another city or even to her own homeland if the marriage occurred elsewhere. The Hanafi school allows travel only up to a short distance (less than sixteen farsakhs) according to the three imams, except to a non-Muslim land. However, if the two places are so close that the father can visit and return within a day, she may take the child freely. This ruling applies specifically to the mother; even the grandmother needs the father's permission to move the child.

Key Terms
الحضانة (al-ḥaḍānah): The right to physically care for and raise a young child, typically until the child no longer needs a female custodian.
الوطن (al-waṭan): One's homeland or original place of residence. In this context, it refers to the town/city where the mother originally comes from.
الفرسخ (al-farsakh): A unit of distance approximately 5-6 km (or a mile and a half to two miles depending on the school). Sixteen farsakhs is about 80-100 km.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Under Hanafi law, when may a divorced mother travel with her child after her 'idda?
A) Anytime she wishes, as long as she informs the father
B) Only to her homeland where the marriage took place, or to a very close distance
C) Only if the father agrees in writing
D) She may travel up to 16 farsakhs without restriction

Key Takeaway
A divorced mother's right to travel with her child is limited to her homeland where the marriage took place, to protect the father's access.

Prepaid Maintenance Not Refunded Upon Death
Ruling on non-refund of prepaid maintenance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on prepaid maintenance (al-mu'ajjalah) upon death of a spouse and explain why it is non-refundable.

Core Idea
Prepaid maintenance is not refunded if either spouse dies — the advance is a completed right.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq states a clear ruling regarding maintenance paid in advance. Understanding this principle is crucial for dealing with marital financial obligations and the effects of death on prepaid rights.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is giving prepaid maintenance (al-mu'ajjalah) to the wife before it is due. The ruling is that it is not refunded upon death of either spouse.
The text uses 'لا ترد' (it is not refunded) which indicates a definitive ruling.
2
Identify the triggering condition
The condition that triggers non-refund is the death of either spouse. The text says 'بموت أحدهما' (upon death of one of them).
3
Understand the nature of prepaid maintenance
Prepaid maintenance (mu'ajjalah) is given in advance, e.g., for a full year. Once given, it becomes a completed right for the wife.
The text gives an example: 'أسلفها نفقة سنة' (he advanced her maintenance for a year).
4
Note the absence of refund
The text explicitly states that the prepaid amount is not reclaimed ('لا يسترد ذلك'). This is the operative ruling.
This applies whether the husband dies first or the wife dies first.
5
Consider the legal basis
The non-refund is based on the idea that the maintenance became an established right of the wife once paid, and death does not retroactively invalidate it.
This is a Hanafi position; other schools may differ on refundability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا تردّ المعجلة. أي لا ترد المعجلة بموت أحدهما بأن أسلفها نفقة سنة مثلاً ثم مات أحدهما لا يسترد ذلك.
And prepaid (maintenance) is not refunded. Meaning: prepaid is not refunded upon death of one of them, such as if he advanced her maintenance for a year, then one of them died, that is not reclaimed.
Source pages p. 528

Commentary
This passage states a ruling: if a husband gives his wife maintenance in advance, and then either spouse dies, the prepaid amount is not refunded. The commentary explains that if he gave her a year's maintenance as a loan or advance, and then either dies, the surviving party does not reclaim it. This is because the maintenance was given as a right, and death does not necessitate its return.

Key Terms
المعجلة (al-mu'ajjalah): Maintenance paid in advance before it is due

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Yes, she must refund the unused portion.
B) No, it is not refunded.
C) It depends on the contract.
D) Only if the husband's heirs demand it.

Key Takeaway
Prepaid maintenance is a completed right and is not refunded upon death of either spouse.

Sale of Slave for Wife's Maintenance
Ruling on selling a slave for wife's maintenance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a slave can be sold to cover his wife's maintenance debt and identify which types of slaves are excluded from this ruling.

Core Idea
A slave's maintenance debt for his wife, if married with permission, can be collected by selling him repeatedly—a unique feature of maintenance debts.

This passage addresses a specific scenario in Hanafi family law: when a full slave (qinn) marries a free woman with his master's permission, her maintenance becomes a debt that attaches to his value. The ruling distinguishes this from other debts and explains exceptions for mudabbar, mukatab, and unauthorized marriages. Understanding this clarifies the interplay between slavery, marriage, and financial obligations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling and its condition
Extract the basic rule: a slave can be sold to pay his wife's maintenance. Note the essential condition: the marriage must be with the master's permission. This condition makes the debt manifest in the master's right.
Without permission, no maintenance obligation arises.
2
Recognize the unique repeated sale rule
Observe that maintenance debt permits repeated sales of the slave, unlike all other debts which only allow a single sale. Identify the thamarah al-khilaf: this is a practical difference from ordinary debt.
Maintenance debt can be enforced multiple times, reflecting its recurring nature.
3
Distinguish among slave types
Classify the slaves: full slave (qinn) is sold; mudabbar and mukatab are not sold under normal circumstances because their value is not available for sale. For a mukatab, if he becomes unable to pay his kitabah, he can then be sold for maintenance.
The category of the slave determines enforceability.
4
Apply the exception for unauthorized marriage
If the slave married without permission, the marriage is invalid and no maintenance is owed. Even if consummated, the dower does not attach to the slave's value during slavery; it is pursued after freedom.
Unauthorized marriage has no legal effect on the master's liability.
5
Analyze the limitation on dower
Note that the dower (mahr) does not become a debt on the slave during slavery because its obligation does not manifest in the master's right due to interdiction. The slave is only pursued after freedom unless the master ransoms him.
Dower is treated differently from maintenance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبيع القن في نفقة زوجته: أي إذا تزوج العبد حرة فنفقتها دين عليه يباع فيها، وهذا إذا تزوجها بإذن المولى؛ لأنها دين وجبت في ذمته؛ لوجود سببه وهو النكاح، وقد ظهر ذلك الوجوب في حق المولى؛ لأنه التزمه بالإذن، فتعلق برقبته كدين التجارة في المأذون ولو اجتمع عليه نفقة أخرى بعد ما بيع مرة، بيع ثانياً، وكذا ثالثاً إلى ما لا يتناهى، وليس من الديون ما يباع فيه مراراً إلا دین النفقة، وغيره من الديون يباع فيه مرة واحدة، فإن أوفى الغرماء، وإلا طولب به بعد الحرية، وإنما قيد بالقن؛ لأن المدبر لا يباع ونفقتها تتعلق بكسبه، وكذا المكاتب إلا عند العجز، فإنه إن عجز يباع في النفقة، أما إذا كان التزوج بغير الإذن فلا نفقة عليهم؛ لأن النكاح لم يصح فلم تجب النفقة، ولو دخل بما لا يباع في المهر أيضاً؛ لأن وجوب المهر لم يظهر في حق المولى؛ لكونه محجوراً عليه، وإنما يطالب بعد الحرية إلا أن يفديه المولى، ولو مات سقط، وكذا إذا قتل على الصحيح.
And the sale of a slave for his wife's maintenance: i.e., if a slave marries a free woman, her maintenance is a debt upon him, he is sold for it. This is if he married her with the master's permission, because it is a debt that became obligatory in his liability due to the existence of its cause, which is marriage, and that obligation became manifest concerning the master because he undertook it by permission, so it attaches to his neck (value) like a trade debt for the authorized slave. Even if another maintenance accumulates upon him after he was sold once, he is sold a second time, similarly a third time ad infinitum. There is no debt for which one is sold multiple times except maintenance debt; other debts are sold for once. If he satisfies the creditors, otherwise he is pursued after freedom. It is restricted to a full slave because a mudabbar is not sold and her maintenance attaches to his earnings; likewise a mukatab except when unable, then if he is unable he is sold for maintenance. But if the marriage was without permission, there is no maintenance upon them because the marriage was not valid, so maintenance is not obligatory. Even if consummated, he is not sold for the dower either, because the obligation of dower did not become manifest concerning the master due to his interdiction; rather he is pursued after freedom unless the master ransoms him. If he dies, it falls; likewise if he is killed according to the correct view.
Source pages p. 528

Commentary
This passage explains the rule that a slave who marries a free woman with his master's permission incurs a debt of maintenance for her. This debt attaches to his value, so he can be sold to pay it, and if maintenance accumulates again, he can be sold repeatedly, which is unique to maintenance debts. The rule applies to a full slave (qinn), not a mudabbar or mukatab under normal conditions, because their value is not available for sale. If the slave married without permission, the marriage is invalid and no maintenance is owed. The dower is also not recoverable from the slave during slavery; only after freedom.

Key Terms
القن (al-qinn): A full slave (not mudabbar or mukatab) who can be sold
التجارة في المأذون (al-tijārah fī al-ma’dhūn): Trade debts of a slave authorized to trade, which attach to his value
المدبر (al-mudabbar): A slave promised freedom upon the master's death; his value is not available for sale
المكاتب (al-mukātab): A slave under a contract of manumission (kitabah); he owns his earnings

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Under what condition can a slave be sold for his wife's maintenance according to the Hanafi ruling?
A) Only if he married without permission.
B) Only if he is a mudabbar.
C) If he married a free woman with master's permission.
D) Only if he is a mukatab.

Key Takeaway
A full slave can be sold repeatedly to cover his wife's maintenance only if his marriage was with the master's permission; this rule is unique to maintenance debts.

Maintenance of a Married Female Slave by Tabwiyah
Ruling on when maintenance becomes due for a married female slave

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition for maintenance of a married female slave and identify when it is due, when it ceases, and exceptions.

Core Idea
Maintenance of a married female slave is due only when the master provides separate quarters (tabwiyah) and does not employ her.

This passage clarifies a specific rule in Hanafi family law regarding the master's obligation to maintain his married female slave. The key is the act of tabwiyah, which establishes confinement (ihtibas) and shifts responsibility from the master to the husband. Understanding this condition prevents common misconceptions about when maintenance is due.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject and ruling
Recognise that the passage addresses the maintenance (nafaqah) of a married female slave. The ruling: it is due only upon tabwiyah.
2
Define tabwiyah
Tabwiyah means the master vacates a separate dwelling for the slave and her husband, and does not employ her for his own work, thereby realizing confinement (ihtibas).
Both components (separate quarters and non-employment) are required.
3
Determine the effect of employment
If the master employs the female slave after tabwiyah, maintenance ceases because confinement is lost.
Employment after tabwiyah invalidates the obligation.
4
Note exceptions and disputes
If the master marries his female slave to his own male slave, maintenance is on the master even without tabwiyah. Also, if tabwiyah occurs after divorce and not before, she has no maintenance, contrary to Zufar.
These are specific rules within the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونفقة الأمة المنكوحة إنما تحب بالتبوية. تفسير التبوية أن يخلى المولى بينها وبينه في منزله ولا يستخدمها؛ لأنه حينئذ يتحقق الاحتباس، وإن استخدمها بعد التبوية سقطت نفقتها؛ لفوات الاحتباس ولا فرق فيه بين أن يكون زوجها حراً أو عبداً أو مكاتباً أو مدبراً، ولو بوأها بعد الطلاق ولم يكن بوأها قبله فلا نفقة لها خلافاً لزفر، وإن زوج أمته من عبده فنفقتها على المولى، بوأها منزلاً أو لا.
And the maintenance of a married female slave is due only by tabwiyah (providing separate quarters). The explanation of tabwiyah is that the master vacates between her and him in his home and does not employ her, because then confinement (ihtibas) is realized. If he employs her after tabwiyah, her maintenance falls due to loss of confinement. There is no difference in this whether her husband is free, a slave, a mukatab, or a mudabbar. If he provided quarters after divorce and had not done so before, she has no maintenance, contrary to Zufar. And if he marries his female slave to his male slave, her maintenance is on the master, whether he provided quarters or not.
Source pages p. 528

Commentary
This passage clarifies that a married female slave's maintenance becomes due only when the master provides her with a separate dwelling (tabwiyah), meaning he vacates a place for her and her husband and refrains from employing her. If he then employs her, maintenance ceases. This rule applies regardless of the husband's status. If the master marries his own slaves to each other, maintenance is still on the master even without separate quarters.

Key Terms
التبوية (al-tabwiyah): The act of the master providing a married female slave with a separate dwelling, vacated of his own use, and not employing her
الاحتباس (al-ihtibas): Confinement; the condition where the female slave is effectively kept for her husband's service, realized by tabwiyah
أمة منكوحة (amah mankuhah): A female slave who is married

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When does maintenance become due for a married female slave in Hanafi law?
A) Immediately upon marriage.
B) Only when the master provides her separate quarters (tabwiyah).
C) Only if she is pregnant.
D) Only if the husband is free.

Key Takeaway
Maintenance of a married female slave in Hanafi law is conditional upon the master providing separate quarters (tabwiyah) and not employing her.

Dwelling Requirement and Visitation Rights for Wife
Ruling that husband must provide a dwelling separate from both families, and visitation rights

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the wife's right to a separate dwelling, cite the Quranic evidence, and apply the exceptions and visitation rules.

Core Idea
The wife has a right to a separate dwelling from both families, proven by the Quranic imperative.

This passage from a Hanafi fiqh commentary explains that the wife's right to a dwelling is a distinct obligation from maintenance. The proof is the Quranic command 'Lodge them where you lodge.' The commentary expands on what constitutes a proper dwelling and the husband's obligations regarding privacy and family visits.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its basis
State that dwelling (sukna) is wajib for the husband. Note that the commentator singles it out because it has unique rulings. The proof is Surah al-Talaq 65:6, which commands lodging.
The imperative form indicates obligation in Hanafi usul.
2
Understand the scope of the dwelling
Recognize that the dwelling must be separate from both the husband's family and the wife's family, to prevent harm and protect her belongings.
The text says 'empty of his family and her family'.
3
Identify exceptions
Note that if the wife consents to share, it is permissible. Also, the husband may house his female slave with her due to need for service.
Consent waives the right; the slave exception is based on necessity.
4
Determine sufficiency of a separate unit
Understand that if the husband provides a separate room with amenities and a lock, it suffices even if within the same building.
The objective is achieved with privacy and security.
5
Apply the visitation rights
The husband cannot prevent the wife's family from visiting and speaking, but may restrict overnight stays. The wife may visit parents and receive visits from close relatives weekly.
This prevents cutting of kinship ties.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والسكنى: عطف على قوله في أول الباب: تجب النفقة أي وتجب السكنى أيضاً للزوجة على الزوج، وإنما أفردها بالذكر مع أن اسم النفقة يعمها؛ لأن لها حكماً يخصها، أما وجوب السكني؛ فلأن الله تعالى أوجبه مقروناً بالنفقة حيث قال: ﴿أَسْكِنُوهُنَّ مِنْ حَيْثُ سَكَنتُمْ (الطلاق: ٦)، فإذا وجب حقاً لها فليس للزوج أن يشرك غيرها في حقها؛ لأنها تتضرر به وهو المراد بقوله: "في بيت خال عن أهله وأهلها"؛ لأنها تخاف على متاعها، ويمنعها عن المعاشرة مع زوجها، وعن الاستمتاع، إلا أنها إذا اختارت فقد رضيت بانتقاص حقها، فلو أسكن معها أمته ليس لها أن تمتنع من ذلك؛ لأنه يحتاج إلى الاستخدام فلا يستغني عنها. ولو أخلى لها بيتاً من دار، وجعل لها مرافق، وقفلاً على حدة كفاها؛ لحصول المقصود بذلك، فإن اشتكت من الزوج الإيذاء بسوء العشرة، وعلم القاضي بذلك أو أخبره عدول نهاه عن ذلك، وعليه أن يسكنها عند جيران صالحين والسكنى مصدر سكن الدار إذا أقام أو اسم بمعنى الإسكان والمراد بالبيت بقدر حالهما كطعام وكسوة. وأهلها : أي وعن أهل الزوجة إلا أن تختار ذلك. ولهم النظر إلخ أي ليس للزوج أن يمنع أهلها من النظر إليها والكلام معها في أي وقت شاءوا؛ لأن في ذلك قطيعة الرحم وليس فيه ضرر له ولكن له أن يمنعهم من المكث عندها، وقيل: لا يمنعها من الخروج إلى الوالدين، ولا يمنعهم من الدخول عليها في كل جمعة، وفي غيرهما من المحارم في كل عام هو الصحيح.
And dwelling: it is a conjunction to his statement at the beginning of the chapter: 'Maintenance is obligatory', meaning that dwelling is also obligatory for the wife upon the husband. He singled it out for mention even though the term maintenance includes it, because it has a specific ruling. As for the obligation of dwelling, because Allah the Exalted made it obligatory alongside maintenance where He said: 'Lodge them where you lodge' (65:6). So when it is obligatory as a right, the husband may not associate others with her in her right because she is harmed by that. That is the meaning of his statement: 'in a house empty of his family and her family' because she fears for her belongings, and it prevents her from cohabitation with her husband and enjoyment. However, if she chooses, she has consented to the diminution of her right. If he houses his female slave with her, she may not refuse that because he needs the service and cannot do without her. If he vacates a house for her from the dwelling and provides amenities and a separate lock, it suffices her because the objective is achieved. If she complains of harm from the husband's bad company, and the judge knows that or trustworthy people inform him, he forbids him from that, and he must lodge her with righteous neighbors. 'Sukna' is the masdar of 'sakan ad-dar' if one resides or a noun meaning lodging. The intended meaning of 'house' is commensurate with their status like food and clothing. 'And her family' means: and away from the wife's family unless she chooses that. 'And for them the sight etc.' means: the husband may not prevent her family from looking at her and speaking with her at any time they wish, because that causes cutting of kinship ties and there is no harm to him. But he may prevent them from staying overnight. It is said: he may not prevent her from going out to her parents, nor prevent them from entering upon her every Friday, and for other relatives every year; that is the correct view.
Source pages p. 528

Commentary
This passage establishes that the husband must provide a separate dwelling for his wife, distinct from his family and her family, based on the Quranic verse commanding lodging. The wife has a right to privacy and protection of her belongings. However, if she consents, it is permissible. The husband may not prevent her family from visiting and speaking, but he can limit overnight stays. The dwelling must be appropriate to their status.

Key Terms
السكنى (al-suknā): The right of the wife to a separate dwelling from the husband's and her family, of a standard appropriate to their status

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the basis for the obligation of providing a separate dwelling for the wife?
A) Consensus of scholars
B) The Quranic verse 'Lodge them where you lodge'
C) The saying of Abu Hanifa
D) The customs of the people

Key Takeaway
The husband must provide a separate dwelling for his wife based on the Quranic command, and the wife retains rights to privacy and family visitation.

8 min
Imam Zufar's View on Maintenance from an Absent Husband's Deposit
Ruling on maintenance from absent husband's property held by custodian

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on maintenance for an absent husband's dependents and state the mufta bihi position per Imam Zufar.

Core Idea
Wife of absent husband: borrow on his credit, do not take directly from his deposit.

When a husband is absent and his property is with a custodian, the judge may need to ensure maintenance for his dependents. The Hanafi school had an internal dispute: Abu Hanifa allowed direct provision from the property, but Imam Zufar ordered the woman to borrow instead, deeming it more beneficial. This lesson unpacks the reasoning and the operative fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and parties
Understand the scenario: an absent husband, a custodian holding his property, and dependents (wife, minor children, needy parents). The custodian acknowledges the property and marriage.
The property must be of the same type as the maintenance right (currency, food, clothing).
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and its basis
Abu Hanifa holds that the judge can impose maintenance directly from the property because the acknowledgment establishes the right and maintenance is obligatory.
The custodian's acknowledgment is accepted concerning himself, so it extends to the absent.
3
State Imam Zufar's position and its reasoning
Zufar argues that the woman should be ordered to borrow on the husband's behalf, not given from the deposit, because it is more beneficial and avoids potential complications (e.g., husband already paid, wife estranged).
This is considered 'arf (beneficial custom).
4
Identify the mufta bihi and practical consequence
The operative fatwa follows Zufar's view. The practical difference: under Abu Hanifa, the woman receives directly; under Zufar, she must borrow and the debt is on the husband.
This is one of six issues where fatwa is given by Zufar's statement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفرض لزوجة الغائب وطفله وأبويه في مال له عند من يقر به وبالزوجية، ويؤخذ كفيل منها: وفرض على صيغة المجهول أي الإنفاق يعني يفرض القاضي النفقة. لزوجة الغائب إلخ : أي إذا غاب الرجل وله مال في يد رجل يعترف بالمال وبالزوجية، فرض القاضي في ذلك المال نفقة زوجته الغائب وولده الصغار ووالديه وكذا إذا علم القاضي بذلك ولم يعترف به ذو اليد؛ لأنه لما أقر بالزوجية والوديعة فقد أقر أن حق الأخذ لها، وإقرار صاحب اليد مقبول في حق نفسه، ولو أنكر أحد الأمرين، إما النسب أو الزوجية أو المال لا تقبل بينة المرأة؛ لأن المودع ليس بخصم في إثبات الزوجية عليه، ولا المرأة خصم في إثبات حقوق الغائب، فإذا ثبت في حقه تعدى إلى الغائب وقال زفر لا يقضى بإعطائها من مال الرديعة وتؤمر بالاستدانة عليه وهذا أرفق بالناس، وبه يفتى، وهذا من الست الذي يفتى فيها بقول زفر. وهذا كله إذا كان المال من جنس حقهم من النقود أو الطعام أو الكسوة، أما إذا كان خلاف الجنس فلا تفرض النفقة فيه؛ لأنه يحتاج إلى القضاء بالقيمة أو إلى البيع وكل ذلك لا يجوز على الغائب، والتبر بمنزلة الدراهم في هذا الحكم؛ لأنه يصلح قيمة للمضروب. وأبويه أي أبوي الغائب المحتاجين ولو قادرين على الكسب. في مال له أي مال كائن للغائب من جنس حقهم. من يقرّ به: أي شخص يقر بالمال ويعترف أن هذا المال للغائب. بالزوجية: أي يعترف أيضاً بالزوجية بأن يقول: أعلم أنها زوجة فلان الغائب وكذا ينبغي أن يعترف بالنسب في حق الأطفال سواء كان المال أمانة أو ديناً. ويؤخذ كفيل منها: أي إذا أعطيت المرأة من مال الزوج الذي كان وديعة عند رجل يؤخذ من المرأة ضامن احتياطاً لجواز أنه قد كان عجل لها النفقة أو كانت ناشزة أو مطلقة قد انقضت عدتها، وتحلف بالله مع التكفيل أيضاً احتياطاً، ولا يقضى بنفقة في مال الغائب إلا لهؤلاء المذكورين وهم الزوجة والوالدان والأولاد الصغار والأولاد الكبار الإناث والكبار الذكور من لا قدرة له على الكسب؛ لأن القضاء على الغائب لا يجوز، ونفقة هؤلاء واجبة قبل القضاء، فيكون القضاء إعانة لفتوى القاضي ولو لم يقر الذي في يده المال ولم يعلم القاضي فأقامت المرأة البينة ليقضى لها في مال الغائب ، أو لتؤمر بالاستدانة لا تسمع بينتها؛ لأنه قضاء على الغائب، وقال زفر: تسمع بينتها ولا يقضى بالنكاح، وتعطى النفقة ماله إن كان له مال ولا تؤمر بالاستدانة، من قالت و به وعليه عمل القضاة اليوم وبه يفتى
And the judge imposes (maintenance) for the absent husband's wife, his child, and his parents from property of his in the possession of one who acknowledges it and acknowledges the marriage, and a guarantor is taken from her. 'Farada' is passive, meaning expenditure, i.e., the judge imposes maintenance. 'For the absent husband's wife etc.': if a man is absent and has property in the hand of a man who acknowledges the property and the marriage, the judge imposes from that property maintenance of his wife, his minor children, and his parents. Similarly, if the judge knows that without the possessor's acknowledgment, because when he acknowledged the marriage and the deposit, he acknowledged that the right to take it is hers. The possessor's acknowledgment is accepted concerning himself. If he denies one of the two matters—either lineage, marriage, or the property—the woman's evidence is not accepted because the depositary is not a litigant in proving marriage against him, nor is the woman a litigant in proving the absent husband's rights. So if it is established concerning him, it extends to the absent. Zufar said: 'The judge should not decree giving her from the deposit property; rather she should be ordered to borrow on his behalf, and that is more beneficial for the people, and that is the fatwa.' This is from the six (issues) where fatwa is given by Zufar's statement. All this is if the property is of the same type as their right: currency, food, or clothing. If it is of a different type, maintenance is not imposed from it because it requires a judgment of value or sale, none of which is permissible concerning the absent. Uncoined gold is like dirhams in this ruling because it can be used as value for struck coin. 'And his parents' means the absent husband's parents who are needy, even if able to earn. 'From property of his' means property belonging to the absent of the same type as their right. 'From one who acknowledges it' means a person who acknowledges the property and admits that this property belongs to the absent. 'Acknowledges the marriage' also acknowledges the marriage by saying 'I know she is the wife of so-and-so absent'. Similarly, he should acknowledge lineage for children, whether the property is a trust or a debt. 'And a guarantor is taken from her' means: when the woman is given from the husband's property that was a deposit with a man, a guarantor is taken from her as a precaution in case he had already given her maintenance or she was recalcitrant (nashizah) or divorced whose iddah had expired. She also swears by Allah along with the guarantor as a precaution. No maintenance is decreed from the absent's property except for these mentioned: the wife, the parents, minor children, adult daughters, and adult sons who are incapable of earning, because judgment against the absent is not allowed. The maintenance of these is obligatory before the judgment, so the judgment is an aid to the judge's fatwa. If the possessor of the property does not acknowledge and the judge does not know, and the woman brings evidence to be decreed maintenance from the absent's property or to be ordered to borrow, her evidence is not heard because it would be a judgment against the absent. Zufar said: 'Her evidence is heard, and he does not decree the marriage, and she is given maintenance from his property if he has property, and she is not ordered to borrow.' It is said: 'And upon it is the practice of judges today, and it is the fatwa.'
Source pages p. 529

Commentary
This passage explains the procedure when a husband is absent and has property held by someone. The judge can allocate maintenance for the wife, minor children, and needy parents from that property, provided the custodian acknowledges both the property and the marriage. If the custodian denies, the woman's evidence is not accepted because the custodian is not a proper litigant. Imam Zufar (a Hanafi scholar) held a different view: the woman should not be given from the deposit but should borrow on the husband's credit, and this is considered more practical and is given as fatwa in six specific issues. The maintenance is limited to the same type as the right (currency, food, clothing). A guarantor and oath are taken from the woman as precaution. Zufar's view is followed in practice today.

Key Terms
كفيل (kafīl): A guarantor taken from the wife as precaution when she receives maintenance from the absent husband's property, in case the husband had already paid or she is not entitled.
الوديعة (al-wadīʿah): A trust deposit; property left with a custodian for safekeeping.
الست (al-sitt): Refers to six specific issues where fatwa is given according to Zufar's view.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, whose view is relied upon for fatwa regarding maintenance from an absent husband's deposit?
A) Abu Hanifa
B) Abu Yusuf
C) Imam Zufar
D) Imam Muhammad

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fatwa, a woman whose husband is absent should borrow on his credit for maintenance, not take directly from his deposit held by a custodian.

Maintenance and Dwelling for the Divorced Woman in ʿIddah
Ruling on maintenance and dwelling for a woman in ʿiddah of divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on maintenance for a woman in ʿiddah of divorce, explain the condition of residence, and identify when the right is lost.

Core Idea
Every divorced woman in ʿiddah deserves maintenance – but only while she stays in her ʿiddah home.

In Hanafi fiqh, the waiting period (ʿiddah) carries financial rights for the woman. This lesson clarifies that both revocable and irrevocable divorces obligate the husband to provide maintenance and housing, regardless of pregnancy. However, the woman's own conduct – leaving the home without valid excuse – can forfeit those rights. Understanding this balance of obligation and condition is key for proper application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its general ruling
The text states that maintenance (nafaqah) and dwelling (suknah) are obligatory for a woman in ʿiddah of divorce. Note that this extends to all divorced women, not only those in a revocable divorce.
The phrase 'ولمعتدة الطلاق' opens the ruling.
2
Recognize the scope of the ruling
The obligation applies whether the divorce is revocable (rajʿī) or irrevocable (bāʾin), and whether the woman is pregnant or not. No exception is made for these factors.
This is a broader ruling than some other schools.
3
Locate the condition for entitlement
The text specifies a condition (shart): she must stay in the ʿiddah home. If she leaves (even for a short time) without a valid legal excuse, she becomes recalcitrant (nāshizah) and loses her right to maintenance.
The condition is about her residence, not about the divorce type.
4
Understand the consequence of violation
Leaving without excuse makes her nāshizah; the immediate effect is that she no longer deserves maintenance. The dwelling right may also be affected.
This aligns with the general Hanafi principle that a nāshizah wife forfeits maintenance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولمعتدة الطلاق: أي تجب النفقة والسكنى أيضاً لمعتدة إلخ سواء كان بائناً أو رجعياً. عطف على قوله: الثلاثة للزوجة" في أول الباب، أي وتجب النفقة لمعتدة الطلاق سواء كان رجعياً أو بائناً، وسواء كانت حاملاً أو لا، ويشترط في استحقاق النفقة أن تلازم بيت العدة حتى لو خرجت زماناً عن غير عذر شرعي صارت ناشزة، ولا تستحق النفقة،
And for the woman in 'iddah of divorce: i.e., maintenance and dwelling are also obligatory for the woman in 'iddah, whether the divorce is irrevocable (bā'in) or revocable (raj'ī). It is a conjunction to his statement: 'the three for the wife' at the beginning of the chapter, i.e., maintenance is obligatory for the woman in 'iddah of divorce, whether revocable or irrevocable, and whether she is pregnant or not. It is a condition for entitlement to maintenance that she stay in the 'iddah home; if she leaves for a time without a valid excuse, she becomes recalcitrant (nāshizah) and is not entitled to maintenance.
Source pages p. 529

Commentary
This passage states that a woman in the waiting period after divorce is entitled to both maintenance and a dwelling. This applies to all types of divorce (revocable and irrevocable) and regardless of pregnancy. However, she must remain in the designated 'iddah home. If she leaves without a valid excuse, she forfeits her right to maintenance.

Key Terms
المعتدة (al-mu'taddah): The woman observing the waiting period (ʿiddah) after divorce or death of husband
الناشزة (al-nāshizah): A woman who leaves the marital home without valid excuse, thereby forfeiting her right to maintenance
النفقة (al-nafaqah): Maintenance, including food, clothing, and lodging

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what happens if a woman in ʿiddah of divorce leaves her home without a valid excuse?
A) She still receives maintenance but loses her dwelling.
B) She becomes nāshizah and loses her right to maintenance.
C) She must be brought back by force.
D) She loses both maintenance and her right to remarry.

Key Takeaway
A divorced woman in ʿiddah is entitled to maintenance and housing regardless of the type of divorce, but she must stay in the designated home to keep that right.

Maintenance for the Irrevocably Divorced Non-Pregnant Woman: The Hanafi vs Shafi'i Dispute
Inter-madhhab dispute on nafaqah for mubānah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on maintenance for a mubānah, identify the opposing evidence from the hadith of Fatima bint Qays, and evaluate the Hanafi critique of that hadith.

Core Idea
A divorced woman is entitled to maintenance unless she is nāshiz; pregnancy is not a condition.

This passage addresses a classical dispute over whether a woman divorced irrevocably (mubānah) during her 'iddah is entitled to financial support. The Hanafi school asserts that both maintenance (nafaqah) and housing (sukna) are obligatory, regardless of pregnancy. The Shafi'i, Maliki, and Hanbali schools maintain that only a pregnant woman receives maintenance. The core evidence on both sides—a hadith of Fatima bint Qays and a hadith via 'Umar—will be examined.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
State the Hanafi position (maintenance and housing are wajib) and the opposing position (no maintenance unless pregnant). Note the schools that follow each view.
Hanafi: Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad. Opposing: Shafi'i, Maliki, Hanbali.
2
Examine the opposing evidence
Read the hadith of Fatima bint Qays: the Prophet (peace be upon him) did not give her maintenance or housing. Also the variant that makes an exception for pregnancy.
This is the primary proof for the non-Hanafi schools.
3
Analyze the Hanafi critique
Identify the three arguments against the hadith: (1) rejection by senior Companions, (2) internal contradictions in the narration, (3) Fatima's own misconduct (nushuz) that forfeited maintenance.
Key point: the Hanafis argue her case is exceptional, not a general rule.
4
Review the Hanafi positive evidence
Consider the general Quranic command (65:1) and the hadith of 'Umar narrated by al-Tahawi and al-Daraqutni, explicitly granting maintenance and housing to a triple-divorced woman.
The Hanafis prioritize this over the specific case of Fatima.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
State the practical consequence: a Hanafi judge must order maintenance; a Shafi'i judge does not unless the woman is pregnant.
This difference affects real-world legal rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب النفقة
لا الموت والمعصية،
فاطمة بنت
وأن تكون معتدة من نكاح صحيح؛ إذ المعتدة من النكاح الفاسد لا نفقة لها، وأن تكون حرة أو أمة بوأها المولى. وقال الشافعي: لا نفقة للمبانة إلا أن تكون حاملاً؛ لما روي أن فاطمة بنت قيس قالت: "طلقني زوجي ثلاثا، ولم يجعل لي رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم سكنى ولا نفقة"، رواه الجماعة إلا البخاري، وعن الشعبي عن قيس عن النبي عليه في المطلقة ثلاثاً قال: "ليس لها نفقة ولا سكنى"، رواه أحمد ومسلم، وفي رواية مسلم أنه عليه قال: "لا نفقة لها إلا أن تكون حاملاً (الحديث)، وبه قال مالك وأحمد.
ولنا قول عمر ه: "لا ندع كتاب ربنا ولا سنة نبينا عليه لقول ،امرأة، لا ندري لعلها حفظت أو نسيت"، رواه مسلم، وفيما روى الطحاوي والدار قطني زيادة قوله: سمعت رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم يقول: "للمطلقة ثلاثاً النفقة والسكنى". وحديث فاطمة لا يجوز الاحتجاج به من وجوه الأول أن كبار الصحابة أنكروا عليها كعمر وابن مسعود وزيد بن ثابت وأسامة بن زيد وعائشة الله حتى قالت لفاطمة فيما رواه البخاري: ألا الله، تتقي وروي قالت: "لا خير لك فيه، ومثل هذا الكلام لا يقال إلا لمن ارتكب بدعة محرمة وفي صحيح مسلم: لما حدث الشعبي عنها بهذا الحديث أخذ الأسود كفاً . بن يزيد من حصى، وحصب به الشعبي، وقال له: "ويلك أتحدث بمثل هذا"، وقال أبو سلمة أنكر الناس عليها فصار منكراً، فلا يجوز الاحتجاج به. والثاني: أنه مضطرب، فإنه جاء: طلقها البتة وهو غائب، وجاء مات عنها، وجاء حين قتل زوجها، وجاء: طلقها أبو عمرو بن حفص، وجاء طلقها أبو حفص بن المغيرة. والثالث: أن نفقتها سقطت بتطويل لسانها على أحمائها، فلعلها أخرجت لذلك، قال الله تعالى: ﴿ولا تُخْرِجُوهُنَّ مِنْ بُيُوتِهِنَّ وَلَا يَخْرُجْنَ إِلَّا أَنْ يَأْتِينَ بِفَاحِشَةٍ مُبَيِّنَةٍ (الطلاق: ۱) أن تفحش على أهل الرجل فتؤذيهم قاله ابن عباس لما ذكره السفاقشي، وفي شرح البخاري: وفي مصحف أبي له إلا أن تفحش عليكم، وعن سعيد بن المسيب لفاطمة تلك امرأة فتنت الناس، كانت لسنة، وعن عائشة ما بمعناه، فعلم بذلك إنما لم يفرض لها رسول الله له لأجل ذلك؛ لأنها تكون به ناشزة، وشرط النفقة أن تكون محبوسة في بيته، والشافعي احتج به ثم ترك العمل به في حق السكني؛ ولأن هذا حكاية حال فلا يمكن الاحتجاج به رمز الحقائق: ٣١٦/١]
Chapter of Maintenance. No maintenance for death and sin. Fatima bint Qays. And that she be in 'iddah from a valid marriage; for the woman in 'iddah from an invalid marriage has no maintenance, and that she be free or a slave whom the master has given permission to marry. Al-Shafi'i said: There is no maintenance for the irrevocably divorced woman unless she is pregnant, due to the report that Fatima bint Qays said: 'My husband divorced me three times, and the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) did not give me housing or maintenance.' Reported by all except al-Bukhari. And from al-Sha'bi from Qays from the Prophet (peace be upon him) concerning the woman divorced three times: 'She has no maintenance and no housing.' Reported by Ahmad and Muslim. In a narration of Muslim, he (peace be upon him) said: 'She has no maintenance unless she is pregnant.' This is the opinion of Malik and Ahmad. Our evidence is the saying of 'Umar (may Allah be pleased with him): 'We will not abandon the Book of our Lord and the Sunnah of our Prophet (peace be upon him) for the saying of a woman; we do not know whether she remembered or forgot.' Reported by Muslim. And in what al-Tahawi and al-Daraqutni narrated, there is an addition: 'I heard the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) saying: "For the woman divorced three times, maintenance and housing."' The hadith of Fatima cannot be used as evidence for several reasons: First, senior Companions disapproved of her, such as 'Umar, Ibn Mas'ud, Zayd ibn Thabit, Usama ibn Zayd, and 'A'isha (may Allah be pleased with them), so much so that 'A'isha said to Fatima, as reported by al-Bukhari: 'Do you not fear Allah?' And it is reported that she said: 'There is no good in it for you.' Such speech is only said to one who commits a forbidden innovation. In Sahih Muslim, when al-Sha'bi narrated this hadith from her, al-Aswad ibn Yazid took a handful of pebbles and threw them at al-Sha'bi and said: 'Woe to you! Do you narrate such a thing?' Abu Salama said: 'People disapproved of her.' So it became objectionable, and thus cannot be used as evidence. Second, it is contradictory: it came that he divorced her absolutely while he was absent, and that he died, and that her husband was killed, and that Abu 'Amr ibn Hafs divorced her, and that Abu Hafs ibn al-Mughira divorced her. Third, her maintenance was dropped because of her lengthening her tongue against her in-laws; perhaps she was expelled for that reason. Allah says: 'Do not expel them from their houses nor let them go out unless they commit a manifest indecency' (65:1). Ibn 'Abbas said: 'that she be obscene against the man's family and harm them.' In Sharh al-Bukhari: 'In the mus-haf of Ubayy: "unless they are obscene to you."' From Sa'id ibn al-Musayyib: 'Fatima is a woman who caused people to be tempted; she was talkative.' From 'A'isha similar words. Therefore it is known that the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) did not prescribe it for her because of that, as she would thereby be nashiz. The condition for maintenance is that she be confined in his house. Al-Shafi'i used this evidence but then abandoned acting on it regarding housing. And because this is a report of a specific case, it cannot be used as evidence. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 3/316)
Source pages p. 530

Commentary
This is an inter-madhhab dispute over whether a woman divorced irrevocably (mubānah) who is not pregnant is entitled to maintenance (nafaqah) during her 'iddah. The Hanafi position, attributed to Abu Hanifa and his disciples, holds that she is entitled to both maintenance and housing (sukna). The opposing position, stated here by al-Shafi'i and followed by Malik and Ahmad, is that she has no maintenance unless she is pregnant, relying on the well-known hadith of Fatima bint Qays. The Hanafi response, presented in the text, argues that the hadith of Fatima is not reliable: first, senior Companions like 'Umar and 'A'isha rejected her claim; second, the narration is contradictory in its details; third, Fatima lost her maintenance because of her own misconduct (fāḥishah). The Hanafis instead rely on the general Quranic command (Quran 65:1) and the hadith narrated by al-Tahawi that the Prophet (peace be upon him) granted maintenance to a triple-divorced woman. The text also notes that al-Shafi'i himself did not consistently apply the hadith, as he still required housing. The Hanafi view is that the condition for maintenance is that the woman be confined in her husband's house for the 'iddah, which applies to all irrevocably divorced women, pregnant or not.

Key Terms
مبانة (mubānah): A woman who has been divorced irrevocably (bā'in), whether by triple divorce or khul' or other causes where the husband cannot take her back without a new marriage.
ناشزة (nāshiz): A wife who is disobedient or rebels against her husband's authority, often by leaving the marital home without legal cause.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, does a woman divorced three times (mubānah) who is not pregnant receive maintenance during her 'iddah?
A) No, maintenance is only for pregnant women.
B) Yes, maintenance and housing are wajib.
C) Yes, but only housing, not food.
D) No, because her 'iddah is not from a valid marriage.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school obligates maintenance and housing for every mubānah during 'iddah regardless of pregnancy, unless she is nashiz.

intermediate
8 min
No Maintenance for Widow or Woman in Sin-Based 'Iddah
Ruling statement excluding two categories from maintenance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two categories of women excluded from maintenance during 'iddah and explain the Hanafi rationale for each exception.

Core Idea
Not all 'iddah earns maintenance—widows and those separated by their own sin lose the right.

In Hanafi family law, the general rule is that a husband must provide maintenance for his wife during her 'iddah (waiting period) after a revocable or irrevocable divorce. However, this duty has exceptions. The text before us states clearly that two categories of women receive no maintenance: the widow (whose husband has died) and the woman whose 'iddah resulted from a separation caused by her own sin (e.g., apostasy or a prohibited act). Let us unpack the reasoning behind these exceptions and the key distinctions that preserve maintenance in other cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Two Exceptions
Locate in the Arabic text the two cases where maintenance is denied: after death and after a sin-based separation. Note the precise wording: 'lā nafaqah li-mutawaffā 'anhā zawjuhā' and 'lā nafaqah li-mu'taddat al-ma'siyah'.
These are named exceptions to the general rule that 'iddah entitles a woman to maintenance.
2
Understand the Rationale for the Widow
The text says her confinement ('ihtibās) is for the right of the Law (ḥaqq al-shar'), not for the husband's right. Her 'iddah (four months and ten days) is a mourning period prescribed by Allah, not a period for the husband to retain her. Therefore, the husband's estate does not owe maintenance.
Contrast with a divorcee whose 'iddah is to ensure the womb is free and allows reconciliation; maintenance is wajib there.
3
Understand the Rationale for Sin-Based 'Iddah
The text explains that a woman whose own sin caused the separation (e.g., apostasy, kissing the stepson before divorce) has confined herself without right. She is likened to a nāshiz (rebellious wife) who forfeits maintenance.
The key is her fault. If the separation is from her side but without sin (e.g., option of puberty), maintenance remains wajib.
4
Distinguish from Cases Where Maintenance Is Given
The text contrasts two scenarios: (a) separation without sin (e.g., khayār al-ʿitq, al-bulūgh, tafrīq due to lack of kafāʿah) – maintenance is wajib because she confined herself for a right; (b) mahr after consummation – still owed despite sin because delivery occurred and was confirmed.
This shows that only the combination of 'iddah + fault removes maintenance; other financial rights remain.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا الموت والمعصية: أي لا نفقة للمتوفى عنها زوجها؛ لأن احتباسها لحق الشرع لا لحق الزوج؛ إذ التربص عبارة عنها لا للفراغ عن براءة الرحم؛ ولهذا لم يشترط الحيض، وتجب قبل الدخول بها، وكذا لا نفقة لمعتدة المعصية أي المعتدة بالفرقة التي وقعت بسبب معصية، جاءت من قبلها مثل الردة وتقبيل ابن الزوج قبل الطلاق؛ لأنها صارت حابسة نفسها بغير حق، فصارت كأنها ناشزة بخلاف المهر بعد الدخول؛ لأنه وجد التسليم من جهتها فيتأكد بالدخول فيجب، وبخلاف الفرقة التي جاءت من قبلها بغير معصية كخيار العتق والبلوغ والتفريق لعدم عدم الكفاءة؛ لأنها حبست نفسها لحق فصار كحبسها لاستيفاء المهر. (مستخلص)
No [maintenance for] death and sin: i.e., there is no maintenance for the woman whose husband has died, because her confinement is for the right of the Law, not for the right of the husband; for the waiting period is an expression of that, not for the clearing of the womb; for this reason menstruation is not required, and it becomes wajib before consummation. Likewise, there is no maintenance for the 'iddah of sin, i.e., the woman in 'iddah due to a separation that occurred because of a sin committed by her, such as apostasy or kissing the husband's son before the divorce; because she has confined herself without right, so she becomes like a nashiz, contrary to the mahr after consummation, because delivery [of her person] occurred on her part, so it is confirmed by consummation and becomes obligatory. And contrary to the separation that occurred from her side without sin, like the option of emancipation and puberty and separation due to lack of kafa'ah, because she confined herself for a right, so it is like her confinement for receiving the mahr. (Mukhtasar)
Source pages p. 530

Commentary
This is a ruling statement explaining two cases where a woman in 'iddah has no right to maintenance. The first is the widow (mutawaffā 'anhā zawjuhā): maintenance is not wajib because her waiting period (iddah of four months and ten days) is for the sake of the Law (shar') to observe mourning, not for the husband's right of retention. Unlike a divorcée's 'iddah, which is to ensure the womb is free, the widow's 'iddah is established without requiring menstruation, and it is even incumbent before consummation. The second is the woman in 'iddah due to her own sin (ma'siyah), such as apostasy or a forbidden act like kissing her stepson before divorce that led to the separation. Such a woman is considered to have confined herself without right, akin to a nashiz (rebellious wife), so she loses maintenance. The text distinguishes this from two situations: (1) if the separation is from her side without sin (e.g., exercising an option after emancipation or puberty, or divorce due to lack of kafa'ah), maintenance is still wajib because she confined herself for a legitimate right; (2) the mahr after consummation is still owed because she delivered her person and consummation confirmed it.

Key Terms
معتدة المعصية (mu'taddat al-ma'siyah): A woman whose 'iddah period results from a divorce that occurred due to her own sinful act, such as apostasy or a prohibited act that caused the divorce.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A woman whose husband dies: does she receive maintenance during her 'iddah according to the Hanafi school?
A) Yes, maintenance is wajib for all widows.
B) No, because her 'iddah is for the right of the Law, not the husband.
C) Yes, but only if she is pregnant.
D) It depends on whether the marriage was consummated.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the right to maintenance during 'iddah is not absolute: it is denied to widows and to women whose 'iddah results from their own sin.
